Divine Transcendence
beginner
5 min
Maturidi's Critique of Jewish and Christian Corruption of Tawhid
Maturidi's criticism of Jewish and Christian claims of divine sonship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Jewish and Christian claims of divine sonship and why he sees it as a corruption of tawhid.

Core Idea
Claiming divine sonship for prophets corrupts the absolute oneness of Allah.

Maturidi emphasizes that the foundation of all heavenly religions is tawhid—the oneness of God. However, both Jews and Christians have attributed sonship to prophets, thereby corrupting this fundamental principle. In this passage, Maturidi explains why such claims undermine the transcendence of Allah and distort true religion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the key terms: تَوْحِيد, اليهود, النصارى, المسيح, عزير, ابن الله.
Pay attention to the flow from principle to critique.
2
Identify the two claims
The passage states that Jews claim Ezra is the son of God, and Christians claim the Messiah is the son of God. Identify these in the Arabic.
3
Understand Maturidi's reasoning
Maturidi explains that attributing transcendent attributes to prophets removes holiness from its original state to a material one, distorting religion. This is the core of his critique.
Reflect on why this is a corruption of tawhid.
4
Reflect on Maturidi's emphasis
Consider Maturidi's severe criticism at the end of the passage. He gives great attention to the lofty station of divinity.
This shows his commitment to preserving divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن المبدأ الأساسي الذي أسست عليه الأديان السماوية هو توحيد الرب، وأنه ليس له شريك ولا ولد؛ غير أن اليهود قالت بأن عزيرًا ابن الله ، وقالت النصارى المسيح ابن الله، فهم بذلك أفسدوا عقيدة التوحيد . فالأوصاف التي تعلو طبيعة البشر وإضافتها إلى الأنبياء أو القادة الدينية تستلزم إخراج القدسية من حالتها الأصلية إلى حالة الطبيعة والمادية، وهذا يستدعى بالتالي تشويه الدين والماتريدي الذي يعطي اهتماما بالغا بالمقام العالي للألوهية، نراه في كتابه في آخر مباحث النبوة بأنه ينتقد انتقادًا شديد اللهجة هؤلاء الذين يدعون بالانتساب إلى السيد المسيح ويدعون أنه ابن الله.
Indeed, the basic principle upon which the heavenly religions are founded is the tawhid of the Lord, and that He has no partner or child; however, the Jews said that Ezra is the son of God, and the Christians said the Messiah is the son of God, so by that they corrupted the creed of tawhid. For the attributes that transcend human nature and their attribution to prophets or religious leaders necessitates removing holiness from its original state to a state of nature and materiality, and this consequently entails distortion of religion. And al-Maturidi, who gives great attention to the lofty station of divinity, we see him in his book at the end of the sections on prophethood severely criticizing those who claim affiliation with the Messiah and claim that he is the son of God.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's critique of Jews and Christians for corrupting tawhid. It begins by stating that the fundamental principle of all heavenly religions is tawhid: God has no partner and no child. However, the Jews claimed that Ezra is the son of God, and the Christians claimed that the Messiah is the son of God. In doing so, they corrupted the creed of tawhid. Maturidi then explains that attributing attributes that transcend human nature to prophets or religious leaders removes holiness from its original transcendent state to a material one, distorting religion. The passage concludes by noting that Maturidi, who highly regards the majesty of divinity, severely criticizes those who claim to follow the Messiah yet claim he is the son of God. This is found at the end of his discussion on prophethood in his book.

Key Terms
توحيد
اليهود
النصارى
المسيح
عزير
ابن الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi accepts the possibility of divine sonship in a metaphorical sense.
Correction: Maturidi categorically rejects any form of sonship attributed to prophets as a corruption of tawhid.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what do Jews and Christians claim that corrupts tawhid?
A) That God has partners
B) That prophets are divine
C) That Ezra and the Messiah are sons of God
D) That the Messiah is a prophet

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms that tawhid is the foundation of religion and that any claim of divine sonship for prophets corrupts this fundamental principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever unconsciously elevate a religious leader, scholar, or even a beloved prophet to a status that rivals Allah's uniqueness? Today, examine your heart: is there any person you regard with such awe that you would be hesitant to correct them if they claimed something contrary to tawhid? Commit to memorizing this passage so you can defend Allah's oneness against any such temptation.
?
The Jews and Christians corrupted tawhid by attaching divine attributes to prophets. In your own life, have you ever 'slipped' into thinking that the intercession of a saint or the status of a scholar is independent of Allah's will? Confess this to Allah right now and renew your commitment to making Him the sole object of your hope and fear.
?
Maturidi says that attributing transcendent attributes to prophets 'necessitates removing holiness from its original state to a state of nature and materiality.' Where in your daily interactions do you treat the sacred as ordinary—for example, by speaking about Allah's names without reverence, or by placing worldly concerns above your prayers? Write down one concrete change you will make today to restore holiness to its proper place.

Allah's Transcendence of Need and Ignorance
Affirms Allah's intrinsic wisdom and absolute independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's wisdom and self-sufficiency are intrinsic and not dependent on external causes, and that need and ignorance are impossible for Him.

Core Idea
Allah's wisdom and knowledge are self-sufficient, free from need and ignorance.

This passage from Imam Maturidi affirms that Allah's attributes of wisdom and knowledge are inherent and not reliant on any external factor. It contrasts human limitations with divine perfection, establishing the impossibility of attributing need or ignorance to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the three divine attributes mentioned: al-Hakim, al-Ghani, al-'Alim.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the three attributes
Find the three attributes and note how each is described as intrinsic and self-sufficient. Ask: what does 'intrinsic wisdom' mean?
3
Understand the contrast with humans
The passage contrasts human deficiencies (ignorance and need) with divine perfection. Why are these impossible for Allah? Relate to the attributes.
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن الله تعالى هو الحكيم من عنده دون الحاجة إلى علة خارجية، وهو الغنى عن جميع الحاجات، والعليم المحيط بكل شيئ. فالجهل والحاجة هما اللذان يبعدان المرء في العالم المحسوس عن القول الصواب والمعاملة الصحيحة؛ وهذا لا يمكن أن يكون موضوع البحث له سبحانه
But God Almighty is the Wise (al-Hakim) from Himself, without needing an external cause, and He is the Self-Sufficient (al-Ghani) from all needs, and the All-Knowing (al-'Alim) encompassing everything. For ignorance and need are the two things that distance a person in the sensible world from correct speech and sound dealing; and this cannot be a subject of research for Him, exalted is He.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage asserts Allah's transcendence. It states that Allah is al-Hakim (the Wise) intrinsically, without any external cause. He is al-Ghani (the Self-Sufficient), free from all needs, and al-'Alim (the All-Knowing), whose knowledge encompasses everything. The passage then explains that ignorance and need are the causes that prevent humans from correct speech and action in the physical world. Since these deficiencies are impossible for Allah, they cannot be attributed to Him or be subjects of research regarding His actions. Thus, Allah's wisdom and knowledge are perfect and self-sufficient.

Key Terms
الحكيم
الغنى
العليم
الجهل
الحاجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine wisdom requires an external purpose or cause.
Correction: Maturidi emphasizes that Allah's wisdom is intrinsic and not dependent on any external cause.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what two things distance a person from correct speech and sound dealing?
Pride and arrogance
Ignorance and need
Wealth and power
Doubt and uncertainty

Key Takeaway
Allah's attributes of wisdom, self-sufficiency, and knowledge are intrinsic and not dependent on any external cause, and He is free from the deficiencies of ignorance and need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself justifying a wrong word or action because you felt you needed something, do you recognize that your need is a sign of your own deficiency? How will you rely on Allah, the Self-Sufficient, to speak and act correctly today?
?
In what area of your life are you pretending to have wisdom from yourself, when really your ignorance is influencing you? Will you admit your ignorance and seek knowledge from the All-Knowing, even if it humbles you?
?
If Allah's wisdom is perfect and needs no external justification, why do you sometimes question His decisions in your life? What would change in your heart if you truly believed His wisdom stands on its own?

Divine Transcendence
beginner
4 min
God’s Oneness: Beyond Number and Comparison
Maturidi argues that true divine oneness entails transcendence over likeness and opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's oneness is not numerical oneness but absolute uniqueness, which necessitates His transcendence over any like or opposite.

Core Idea
God’s oneness is not like the oneness of numbers; it is complete uniqueness.

Imam Maturidi begins by distinguishing the true meaning of divine oneness from the oneness of numbers. He explains that if God were one in a numerical sense, He would be composed of parts, which is impossible. Instead, God's oneness means He is beyond comparison and opposition. This leads to the conclusion that anything attributed to creation, such as body or accident, cannot apply to God. The passage grounds the Islamic creed of tawhid in rational argument.

Guided Path
1
Clarify the meaning of oneness
Read the first part where Maturidi says God's oneness is not numerical oneness. Understand why numerical oneness implies parts and is deficient.
Notice that numerical oneness applies to created things.
2
Understand the consequence of true oneness
Because God is truly one, He must be beyond all likes and opposites. Maturidi shows that having a like or opposite would compromise His divinity and uniqueness.
3
Examine the rational principle
Maturidi gives a rational principle: anything with a like falls under number, and anything with an opposite is subject to annihilation. God, being eternal, cannot have these.
This applies to all created things but not to God.
4
Apply to reject anthropomorphism
Conclude that body, accident, and any attribute of creation cannot be attributed to God. Reflect on the Quranic verse 'There is nothing like Him' (42:11).
This establishes the fundamental principle of tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفي التشبيه عن الله تعالى وإذا ثبت القول بوحدانية الله تعالى والألوهية له - لا على جهة وحدانية العدد، إذ كل واحد في العدد له نصف وأجزاء ـ لزم القول بتعاليه عن الأشباه والأضداد؛ إذ في إثبات الضد نفي إلهيته، وفي التشابه نفي وحدانيته ؛ إذ الخلق كلهم تحت اسم الأشكال والأضداد، وهما علما احتمال الفناء والعدم ونفي التوحيد عن الخلق. والله واحد لا شبيه له دائم قائم لا ضد له ولاند وهذا تأويل قوله : ليس كمثله شيء [الشورى، ١١/٤٢] . وأصل ذلك أن كل ذي مثل واقع تحت العدد، فيكون أقله اثنين، وكل ذي ضد تحت الفناء إذ يهلكه ضده. وعلى ذلك كل شيء سواه، له ضد يفنى به، وشكل يعدله ويصير به زوجا . فحصل تأويل قوله واحد ، أي في العظمة والكبرياء وفي القدرة والسلطان، وواحد بالتوحد عن الأشباه والأضداد؛ ولذلك بطل القول فيه بالجسم والعرض، إذ هما تأويلا شبه الأشياء. وإذ ثبت ذا بطل تقدير جميع ما يضاف إليه من الخلق
Negation of anthropomorphism from God Most High. And when the statement of the oneness of God Most High and divinity belonging to Him is established — not in the way of numerical oneness, for every numerically single entity has a half and parts — it necessarily follows to affirm His transcendence over likes and opposites; for in affirming an opposite is the negation of His divinity, and in similarity is the negation of His oneness; since all creation come under the name of forms and opposites, and they are signs of the possibility of annihilation and non-existence and the negation of oneness from creation. And God is one, having no likeness, eternal, self-subsisting, having no opposite nor equal. And this is the interpretation of His saying: ‘There is nothing like Him’ [Q. 42:11]. The origin of that is that everything having a like falls under number, so its least is two, and everything having an opposite falls under annihilation because its opposite destroys it. Upon that, everything other than Him has an opposite by which it is annihilated, and a like that equals it and with which it becomes paired. So the interpretation of the word ‘one’ is established, i.e., in greatness and magnificence, and in power and sovereignty, and one by being unique from likes and opposites; and therefore the statement about Him being body or accident is invalid, for they are interpretations of the likeness of things. And when this is established, the estimation of everything that is attributed to Him from among creation becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal argument for the negation of anthropomorphism (tashbīh) from God, based on the concept of true divine oneness (tawḥīd). Maturidi begins by clarifying that the oneness of God is not numerical oneness, because every numerically single thing has parts and a half. Instead, God's oneness means He is incomparable and has no opposite.

The reasoning: If God had an opposite, that opposite would challenge His divinity. If He had a likeness, that likeness would compromise His uniqueness. All creatures are characterized by having likes (ashkāl) and opposites (aḍdād), which indicate possibility of annihilation and non-existence. God, being the necessary existent, cannot have these attributes. Maturidi then cites Quran 42:11: 'There is nothing like Him.'

He explains the logical foundation: anything with a like falls under number (at least two), and anything with an opposite is subject to destruction by that opposite. Since God is eternal and self-subsisting, He cannot have a like or opposite. Therefore, the term 'one' for God means unique in greatness, power, and sovereignty, being unique from all likeness and opposition.

Consequently, attributing body or accident (jawhar / ʿaraḍ) to God is invalid because those imply likeness to created things. If this is established, then any attempt to measure or compare God to creation is false.

Key Terms
تشبيه (tashbīh)
ضد (ḍidd)
شبه (shabbah)
وحدانية العدد (waḥdāniyyat al-ʿadad)
جسم (jism)
عرض (ʿaraḍ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's oneness is the same as numerical oneness, like the oneness of a single object.
Correction: Maturidi explains that numerical oneness implies parts and halves, which is not applicable to God. True oneness is absolute uniqueness without any composition or comparison.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the logical consequence of God having an opposite?
A) It would affirm His divinity.
B) It would negate His divinity.
C) It would make Him easier to understand.
D) It would confirm His oneness.

Key Takeaway
God’s oneness means He is absolutely unique, without any likeness or opposite, and all anthropomorphic descriptions are invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever imagine God with human-like qualities? How does Maturidi's argument that God has no likeness challenge that tendency in your heart? What concrete step will you take to remind yourself of His absolute uniqueness when such thoughts arise?
?
If God's oneness is not like numerical oneness, how does that change the way you relate to Him as 'One'? Write down one area in your life where you treat God as if He were just one among many competitors for your attention—then determine a specific action to give Him exclusive priority there.
?
Maturidi says everything other than God has an opposite and perishes. What in your life are you clinging to that will inevitably perish? How does that realization move you to rely more on God alone, starting today?

God's Oneness is Not Numerical Unity
Maturidi proves God's oneness is not numerical and refutes shirk.

Learning Objective
You will understand why Maturidi insists that God's oneness is not like numerical unity, and how this protects divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God’s oneness is unique, not numerical, because numerical unity implies parts and limitations.

Maturidi begins by clarifying that when we say God is One, we do not mean He is one in the sense of a number. Numerical unity involves halves and parts, which would contradict His divinity. This lesson unpacks his argument, showing how affirming God's oneness as non-numerical leads to rejecting any likeness or opposite for Him, and ultimately invalidates describing Him as body or accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic and translation to grasp the overall argument without analysis.
2
Understand the distinction
Notice the key distinction: God's oneness is not numerical. Maturidi explains that numerical unity implies halves and parts, which cannot apply to God.
This is the foundation of the proof.
3
See the consequences
He derives that if God had a likeness or opposite, His divinity or oneness would be negated. See how this leads to His transcendence over all creation.
4
Reflect on the interpretation of the Quranic verse
Maturidi uses the verse 'There is nothing like Him' to support his argument. Consider how he interprets 'One' in terms of majesty, power, and uniqueness.
5
Apply the conclusion
He concludes that describing God as body or accident is invalid because those are categories of created things. This guards against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفي التشبيه عن الله تعالى
وإذا ثبت القول بوحدانية الله تعالى والألوهية له - لا على جهة وحدانية العدد، إذ كل واحد في العدد له نصف وأجزاء ـ لزم القول بتعاليه عن الأشباه والأضداد؛ إذ في إثبات الضد نفي إلهيته، وفي التشابه نفي وحدانيته؛ إذ الخلق كلهم تحت اسم الأشكال والأضداد، وهما علَما احتمال الفناء والعدم ونفي التوحيد عن الخلق. والله واحد لا شبيه له دائم قائم لا ضد له ولاند وهذا تأويل قوله : ليس كمثله شيء [الشورى، ١١/٤٢]. وأصل ذلك أن كل ذي مثل واقع تحت العدد، فيكون أقله اثنين، وكل ذي ضد تحت الفناء إذ يهلكه ضده. وعلى ذلك كل شيء سواه، له ضد يفنى به، وشكل يعدله ويصير به زوجا. فحصل تأويل قوله واحد، أي في العظمة والكبرياء وفي القدرة والسلطان، وواحد بالتوحد عن الأشباه والأضداد؛ ولذلك بطل القول فيه بالجسم والعرض، إذ هما تأويلا شبه الأشياء. وإذ ثبت ذا بطل تقدير جميع ما يضاف إليه من الخلق.
The negation of anthropomorphism (tashbīh) from God Most High. And when the statement of the oneness (waḥdāniyya) of God Most High and divinity (ulūhiyya) for Him is established—not on the basis of numerical unity, since every one in number has a half and parts—then it is necessary to state His transcendence (taʿālī) over likenesses (ashbāh) and opposites (aḍdād); for in affirming the opposite is the negation of His divinity, and in similarity (tashābuh) is the negation of His oneness; since all creatures are under the name of forms (ashkāl) and opposites, and they are the signs of the possibility of annihilation and non-existence and the negation of oneness from creation. And God is One, no likeness to Him, Eternal, Self-Subsistent, no opposite to Him nor equal. And this is the interpretation (taʾwīl) of His saying: 'There is nothing like Him' (al-Shūrā 42:11). The basis of that is that everything that has a like falls under number, so its minimum is two, and everything that has an opposite falls under annihilation, for its opposite destroys it. And accordingly, everything other than Him has an opposite by which it perishes, and a form that equals it and makes it a pair. So the interpretation of His saying 'One' is obtained, i.e., in majesty and grandeur and in power and sovereignty, and One by being unique (al-tawaḥḥud) from likenesses and opposites; therefore the statement about Him of body (jism) and accident (ʿaraḍ) is invalid, for they are the interpretations of the similarity of things. And when this is established, the estimation (taqdīr) of all that is attributed to Him from creation is invalidated.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God's oneness is not like numerical unity. Maturidi begins by stating that when the oneness and divinity of God are established—not in the manner of numerical unity, since every numerical unit has a half and parts—then it is necessary to affirm His transcendence over all likenesses and opposites. He argues that affirming an opposite would negate His divinity, and affirming similarity would negate His oneness, because all creatures are subject to forms and opposites, which are marks of possible annihilation and non-existence. Then he explains that God is not like that. He gives the interpretation of the verse 'There is nothing like Him': everything that has a like is under number (minimum two), and everything that has an opposite is subject to annihilation. Thus, 'One' means unique in majesty, power, and sovereignty, and unique by being free from likenesses and opposites. Therefore, describing God as body or accident is invalid because those are categories of created things. The conclusion: once this is established, any attribution of created attributes to God is invalidated.

Key Terms
التشبيه
وحدانية
العدد
الضد
المثل
التوحيد
الجسم
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's oneness is like the number one, a simple numeric unity.
Correction: Numerical unity implies parts, halves, and composition, which are incompatible with divine simplicity and transcendence. God's oneness is unique, not like any created unity.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that God's oneness cannot be numerical unity?
A) Because numerical units have opposites
B) Because every numerical unit has a half and parts
C) Because numerical units are created
D) Because numerical units are multiple

Key Takeaway
God's oneness is not numerical, but unique and transcendent, free from all likenesses and opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking of God as 'one among many' — even if only in your imagination? What would change in your worship if you truly internalized that His oneness is not numerical but absolute and unique?
?
In what subtle ways do you attribute form, size, or location to God in your heart? How can you actively remind yourself that He transcends all such created categories?
?
If everything other than God has an opposite that can destroy it, what does that mean for your attachment to people, possessions, or status? Are you willing to hold them loosely, knowing they are subject to annihilation?

Two Heresies and the Muslim Response
Maturidi identifies two heretical groups and states the Muslim position on God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two heretical groups Maturidi identifies (those who make God an entity and the supporters of matter) and the Muslim affirmation of God's unique essence.

Core Idea
Muslims affirm God's essence and negate all imperfections, rejecting heretics' flawed analogies.

In this passage, Maturidi exposes the stubbornness of anthropomorphists (mushabbiha), which leads to two specific heresies. He then states the correct Muslim position: affirming God's essence and negating all that creation can possess.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two heretical groups
Read the passage and note the two groups Maturidi describes: those who make God an entity (a'yan) and the supporters of matter (as-hab al-hayula). Understand their specific errors.
The first group denies the Maker, the second makes God capable of accidents.
2
Understand the Muslim response
Focus on the sentence beginning 'And the Muslims must necessarily affirm His essence...' Notice that Muslims affirm God's essence (huwiyya) and negate from Him all that others can possess.
This includes negating differentiation in essences, change, increase, and decrease.
3
Reflect on uniqueness
Consider why Maturidi emphasizes that even if some attributes are fixed in creation, they are still from the category of what can possess that. This shows God's attributes are unique and not comparable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويوصف به من الصفات بما يُفهم منه لو أضيف إلى الخلق ووصف به. وبالله التوفيق. وفي ذلك ظهور تعنت المشبهة، وذلك سبب إلحاد من ألحد؛ إنه ظن به ما احتمله الشاهد. فمنهم من جعله أحد الأعيان وأنكر الصانع للعالم وادعى أنه على ما عليه [في الأزل] . ومنهم من صيّره محتملا للحوادث وأنكر حَدَثَه وزعم أن غيره حوادث اعترضت بقوته، وهم أصحاب الهيولى. والمسلمون لزمهم القول بهستيته ضرورة فقالوا [به] ونفوا عنه جميع ما احتمل غيره، إذ احتمل غيره التفاضل في الذوات والاختلاف في الصفات، أو احتمل غيره الاستحالة والتغير بما يتمكن فيه الزيادة والنقصان، وإن كان بعض ذلك ثوابت فهو نوع المحتمل لذلك. على أن ثباته بالتسخير على ما هو عليه من دوام الحركة أو السكون، أو باحتمال التضاد الذي هو آفة الموجود بما فيه احتمال الفناء، ووجود الأشباه له ليبطل عنه صفة الكمال والتمام، أو تمكن النهاية له والحد الذي يتوهم معه الأتمّ والأنقص، والأوفر والأقصر، فهذه الوجوه من آيات حدث العالم وأدلة مُحدثه. فلو كان لمحدثه [بعض الصفات] مما به عُرِفَ حَدَث العالم وأن له مُحدثا ليلحقه من ذلك الوجه ما [لحق] غيره.
And He is described with attributes (ṣifāt) such that if they were added to creation and described with them, that would be understood from Him. And with God is success. And in that is the manifestation of the stubbornness of the anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha), and that is the cause of heresy (ilḥād) of whoever became heretical; he assumed about Him what the witnessed (shāhid) allows. Among them are those who made Him one of the entities (aʿyān) and denied the Maker of the world and claimed that it is as it was [in pre-eternity]. And among them are those who made Him capable of receiving accidents and denied its origination (ḥadath) and claimed that other things are accidents that occurred by His power, and they are the supporters of matter (aṣḥāb al-hayūlā). And the Muslims must necessarily affirm His essence (huwiyya) and so they affirmed [it] and negated from Him all that others can possess, since others can possess differentiation in essences (dhāt) and differences in attributes, or others can possess change and alteration including increase and decrease—even if some of that is fixed, it is a type of what can possess that. Moreover, its stability is by subjugation (taskhīr) as it is, whether in continuous motion or rest, or by the possibility of opposition (taḍādd) which is the calamity of existence containing the possibility of annihilation, and the existence of likenesses to it to negate from it the attribute of perfection and completeness, or the possibility of an end and limit by which one imagines the more perfect and the more deficient, the more abundant and the shorter—these aspects are signs of the temporality (ḥadath) of the world and proofs of its Originator. So if its Originator had [some attributes] by which the temporality of the world and that it has an Originator was known, then that which [befalls] others would befall Him from that aspect.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage refutes the anthropomorphists (mushabbiha) and the materialists (aṣḥāb al-hayūlā). Maturidi starts by saying that God is described with attributes (ṣifāt) such that if they were applied to creation, they would be understood from Him—meaning His attributes are unique and not like created attributes. He then notes that this exposes the stubbornness of the anthropomorphists, which leads to heresy: they assume about God what is allowed for the witnessed order (i.e., they liken God to created things). Among these heretics are two groups: (1) those who make God one of the entities (aʿyān) and deny the Maker of the world, claiming the world is eternal; and (2) those who make God capable of receiving accidents and deny the world's origination, claiming that other things are accidents occurring by God's power—these are the supporters of matter (aṣḥāb al-hayūlā). In contrast, Maturidi states that the Muslims must necessarily affirm God's essence (huwiyya) and negate from Him all that others can possess, such as differentiation in essences and attributes, or change and alteration including increase and decrease. He explains that the stability of created things is by subjugation (taskhīr) in motion or rest, or they are subject to opposition and annihilation, or have limits indicating imperfection—all these are signs of the world's temporality and proofs of its Originator. He concludes that if the Originator had any of these attributes (like being subject to change or limits), then He would be subject to the same defects as creation, which is impossible.

Key Terms
المشبهة
الهيولى
الإلحاد
الصفات
الهوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that affirming God's attributes implies similarity to creation.
Correction: Maturidi says that God's attributes are such that if they were added to creation, that would be understood—meaning they are unique and not comparable to created attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what error do the supporters of matter (as-hab al-hayula) commit?
A) They deny the existence of God
B) They claim God is made of matter
C) They make God capable of receiving accidents and deny the world's origination
D) They affirm that God has a body

Key Takeaway
Muslims affirm God's unique essence and negate all creately imperfections, rejecting the analogical reasoning of heretics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even subconsciously, imagined God as having human-like form or emotions? How will you train your mind to reject that false analogy and affirm His unique essence as this lesson teaches?
?
The two heresies both arise from applying worldly logic to God. In what area of your life—such as suffering, unanswered prayers, or justice—do you find yourself judging God by human standards? Will you repent of that and submit to His transcendence today?
?
Affirming God's essence (huwiyya) is necessary, but do you truly feel the weight of its uniqueness in your heart, or do you treat it as a mere intellectual statement? What concrete action can you take this week to deepen your awe of His unfathomable essence?

Signs of the World's Temporality
Maturidi enumerates signs of the world's temporality and concludes its Originator is beyond creately attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments from the signs of the world's temporality that Maturidi presents to prove the existence of God and His uniqueness.

Core Idea
The world's instability, limitations, and dependence point to its createdness and a unique Creator.

After stating the Muslim position, Maturidi provides a series of arguments based on observable features of the world—its subjugation, possibility of opposition, and limits—to demonstrate that the world is temporal and has a single, perfect Originator.

Guided Path
1
List the signs of temporality
Read the passage and identify the four signs Maturidi mentions: stability by subjugation, possibility of opposition, existence of likenesses, and possibility of an end.
Each sign indicates the world's contingency and dependence.
2
Understand each sign's implication
For each sign, think about why it shows the world is not eternal. For example, opposition leads to annihilation, likenesses negate perfection, etc.
These signs are 'ayat' (signs) and 'adilla' (proofs) of the world's temporality.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Note Maturidi's final point: if the Originator had any attributes like creation, He would be subject to the same defects. Therefore, His attributes are unique.

Refuting the Creator as a Natural Cause
Maturidi refutes Creator as natural cause with three arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects labeling Allah as a 'cause' in the natural sense and how he argues that the world must be temporal.

Core Idea
Calling Allah a natural cause contradicts His transcendence and implies the world is eternal.

Maturidi tackles a subtle theological error: describing the Creator as a 'cause' ('illa) of the world in the way natural causes operate. He begins by clarifying what this claim could mean, showing that one interpretation is impossible and the other uses an inappropriate term. Then he offers three arguments that the world's temporal nature is necessary, refuting any notion of eternity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two interpretations
The passage first distinguishes two possible meanings of 'cause': if it means production by nature, it's impossible; if it means bringing into existence, the meaning is correct but the term is corrupt.
Notice how Maturidi separates the concept from the wording.
2
Follow the three aspects
Maturidi gives three reasons why the world must be temporal: contradiction of non-existence, totality by Him, and combination of opposites.
Each aspect builds on the idea that the world requires a temporal beginning.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how this refines our understanding of Allah's agency and the world's contingency. Why does Maturidi reject the term 'cause' even when the meaning is acceptable?
This shows the precision of kalam terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : } وقوله : «الباري علة العالم، إن أراد به کون المصنوع به بالطبع فهو محال، لأنه طريق الاضطرار، ومن ذلك وصفه لا يُحتمل به كون العالم. على أن العالم مُحْدَث مختلف، ومَنْ كون الشيء به بالطبع فهو ذو نوع [واحد] . وإن أراد به أنه يُحدثه فذلك مستقيم، وتسميته علةً فاسدة. وذلك المعنى يوجب كون الشيء بعد أن لم يكن لأوجه. أحدها التناقض ، إذ العدم يوجد، فتقع الحاجة إلى من يوجده، فثبت أن في ذلك وجوب كونه حادثا. والثاني كون كلية العالم به، ومعلوم كون الحادث بعد أن لم يكن. والله أعلم. والثالث أن في ذلك وجود الاجتماع مع التفرق، والحركة مع السكون، والحياة مع الموت، وفي ذلك تناقض وتناف. ثبت أنه كان على التتابع بالأول والثاني ونحوه. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: His saying: 'The Creator is the cause of the world' — if what is meant by it is that the made (thing) is by nature (made by Him), then it is impossible, because it is the way of compulsion, and from that His description does not bear the existence of the world. While the world is temporal and diverse, and whoever makes a thing by nature is of one type. And if what is meant by it is that He brings it into existence, then that is correct, but naming it 'cause' is corrupt. That meaning necessitates the thing being after it was not, for aspects: First, the contradiction, for non-existence exists, so the need arises for one who brings it into existence, and it is established that in that is the necessity of its being temporal. Second, the totality of the world is by Him, and it is known that the temporal is after it was not. And Allah knows best. Third, in that is the existence of harmony with dispersal, motion with stillness, life with death, and in that is contradiction and opposition. It is established that it was in succession by the first and second and the like. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who call the Creator a 'cause' ('illa) of the world in the sense of natural causation. Maturidi first distinguishes two interpretations: if 'cause' means that the world is produced by the Creator by nature, like natural causes produce uniform effects, then it is impossible because the world is temporal and diverse (different kinds), while a natural cause produces only one type of thing. If it means that He brings it into existence, then the meaning is correct but the term 'cause' is inappropriate. Then he gives three reasons why such a natural causation would actually imply the world is temporal, countering the claim of eternity. First, the very need for a cause implies the world's non-existence preceded its existence, which is a contradiction if taken as eternal. Second, the entire world is by Him, and what is produced is known to be temporal. Third, the world contains combinations of opposites (harmony/dispersal, motion/stillness, life/death), which implies it was created in succession, not eternal.

Key Terms
العلة
الباري
العالم
المحال
الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that calling Allah a 'cause' is acceptable and does not affect His transcendence.
Correction: Maturidi shows that calling Allah a cause in the natural sense is impossible because it implies compulsion and uniformity; if it means bringing into existence, the meaning is correct but the term is linguistically corrupt and misleading.

Checkpoint
What is the second of the three 'aspects' (وجوه) that Maturidi gives to show the world is temporal?
A) The contradiction arising from the existence of non-existence
B) The totality of the world being by Him, and the temporal is after non-existence
C) The existence of harmony with dispersal and motion with stillness
D) The fact that natural causes produce only one type

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that applying the term 'natural cause' to Allah is either impossible or inappropriate, and that the world's temporal nature is proven by three rational aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's actions in your life, do you ever treat them as automatic or mechanical—like natural causes—forgetting that every moment is a deliberate act of His will? How can you cultivate awareness in your heart that Allah is not a 'force' but a conscious, personal Creator who brings things into existence at every instant?
?
Maturidi argues that describing Allah as a natural cause diminishes His majesty because it implies uniformity and compulsion. In your own worship, do you sometimes expect Allah to act in predictable, comfortable ways based on your habits, rather than submitting to His free and wise decree? What step can you take this week to surrender to His will even when it defies your expectations?
?
The passage points to the opposites in the world—life and death, harmony and dispersal—as proof that it is created in succession. When you experience pain or loss, do you recognize that this temporal nature is part of Allah's deliberate design, calling you to trust in the One who alone is eternal? How can you turn that moment into an act of reliance on Him?

The Two Meanings of 'Body' and Divine Transcendence
Maturidi states that the term 'body' applied to Allah has two meanings, both inappropriate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects applying the term 'body' to Allah in its literal, witnessed sense, and that this rejection is based on divine transcendence (tanzih).

Core Idea
Allah is not a body because 'body' implies limits and direction, marks of creation.

Maturidi addresses a common theological question: Can we call Allah a 'body'? He clarifies that the term has two distinct meanings. The first meaning refers to what 'body' signifies in the physical world—something with directions, limits, or three dimensions. This meaning cannot apply to Allah, as it implies createdness. The second meaning (not fully preserved in the text) would be a metaphorical or qualified usage. This lesson focuses on the first meaning and its rejection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice that Maturidi begins by stating that ‘body’ has two aspects (وجهين). The first aspect is explained; the second is cut off in the text.
The text is incomplete due to manuscript loss, but the first aspect is complete.
2
Identify the two meanings of ‘body’
Look for where Maturidi says ‘one of them is regarding the quiddity of body…’ This is the first meaning. The second meaning is implied but not given. Understand that the first meaning is the literal, physical sense.
3
Understand why the first meaning is rejected
Maturidi argues that in the witnessed world, ‘body’ entails having directions, limits, or three dimensions. These are proofs of being created (الخلق) and marks of temporality (الحدث). Since Allah is eternal and uncreated, these cannot apply to Him.
This establishes the principle of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [إطلاق لفظ الجسم على الله تعالى] قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم القول بالجسم يخرج على وجهين. أحدهما في مائية الجسم في الشاهد أنه اسم ذي الجهات أو اسم مُحْتَمِل النهايات، أو اسم ذي الأبعاد الثلاثة. فغير جائز القولُ به في الله سبحانه على تحقيق ذلك، لما أدلة الخلق وأمارة الحدث ؛ اذ ذلك
Issue [of applying the term 'body' to Allah the Exalted]. The Shaykh Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Then the saying about 'body' emerges upon two aspects. One of them is: regarding the quiddity of body in the witnessed world, it is a name for that which has directions, or a name for that which admits limits, or a name for that which has three dimensions. So it is not permissible to say it of Allah the Exalted in that reality, because of the proofs of creation and the mark of temporality; for that...
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the term 'body' (الجسم) when applied to Allah. Maturidi begins a new section titled 'Issue of applying the term body to Allah.' He states that the term 'body' can be considered in two ways. The first way is regarding its quiddity (مائية) in the witnessed world: in that context, 'body' means something that has directions, or something that admits limits (end points), or something that has three dimensions. In this sense, it is not permissible to apply the term to Allah, because these attributes are proofs of being created (الخلق) and marks of temporality (الحدث). The text cuts off here, but the point is clear: if 'body' implies spatial extension, direction, or limits, then Allah is not a body, as He transcends such characteristics. The second aspect (which is not fully given in the surviving text) presumably relates to a metaphorical or qualified usage. This passage establishes the Ash'ari/Maturidi principle of divine transcendence (tanzih) regarding anthropomorphic terms.

Key Terms
الجسم (body)
مائية الجسم (quiddity of body)
ذو الجهات (possessor of directions)
محتتمل النهايات (admitting limits)
ذو الأبعاد الثلاثة (possessor of three dimensions)
الخلق (creation)
الحدث (temporality)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that denying Allah is a body means denying His attributes or that He is nothing.
Correction: Maturidi only denies physicality (directions, limits, dimensions), not attributes like knowledge or power.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it not permissible to apply the term 'body' to Allah in its witnessed sense?
A) Because Allah is invisible.
B) Because it implies directions, limits, or dimensions, which are marks of createdness.
C) Because the Quran does not use that term.
D) Because it is too holy to be spoken.

Key Takeaway
The term 'body' in its literal sense implies physical limitations that are incompatible with Allah's transcendence, so it cannot be applied to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah, do you subconsciously picture a being with some form or location? How does this passage challenge that mental image, and what will you do today to purify your understanding of Allah from any trace of physicality?
?
The passage says that having directions, limits, or dimensions are marks of creation. What does it mean for you to worship a Creator who is absolutely beyond these? Does your heart truly accept that, or do you still try to confine Him to your mind's space?
?
If you ever catch yourself thinking of Allah as 'up there' or 'in your heart' as if He occupies a place, how will this passage correct your supplication and your concept of nearness to Him?

6 min
Why 'Body' (Jism) Cannot Be Attributed to Allah
Maturidi gives two reasons why 'jism' cannot be attributed to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments Imam Maturidi uses to reject applying the term 'body' (jism) to Allah, based on both revelation and reason.

Core Idea
Calling Allah 'body' contradicts both reason and revelation.

Imam Maturidi addresses a key theological issue: can we use the term 'body' (jism) for Allah? He gives two clear arguments against it, one based on the implications of the term and its absence from scripture, and another based on the term's lack of a distinct meaning beyond mere existence. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two distinct arguments Maturidi presents.
The passage begins with the first argument and then introduces 'the second'.
2
Identify the two arguments
Identify the first argument (linguistic/revelatory) and the second argument (conceptual). Pay attention to how Maturidi connects each to the rejection of 'jism'.
The first argument focuses on the meaning of 'jism' and lack of scriptural use; the second focuses on its lack of a quiddity.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why Maturidi's reasoning is important. How does it protect divine transcendence? What would be the consequences of accepting 'jism'?
Maturidi warns that if 'jism' were allowed, similar anthropomorphic terms would also become permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى الأجزاء والحدود التي هنّ آيات الحدث، وقد بينا أن ليس كمثله شيء، وفي ذلك إيجاب جعله كأكثر الأشياء. ذلك حسي أو سمعي وإن كان على التسمية به بلا تحقيق ما ذكرنا خرج الاسم عن المعروف به، فبطل تعرف من جهة العقل والاستدلال. وحقه السمع عن الله بأن الجسم من أسمائه، ولم يرد عنه ولا عن أحد ممن أذن لأحد تقليده، فالقول به لا يسع، ولو وسع بالنحت من غير دليل أو عقلي لوسع القول بالجسد والشخص - وكل ذلك مستنكر بالسمع - ولسع القول بكل ما يُسمّى به الخَلْق، وذلك فاسد. وأما الثاني فهو أن يكون الجسم ليست له مائية تُعرف بها سوى الإثبات، فيجوز القول به لو لم يكن يُراد به غيره؛ لكنه لا أحد يجعل الجسم من أسماء الإثبات؛ إذ لا يُسمّى به الأعراض والصفات على احتمالهما اسم الإثبات، لذلك بطل القول به.
The meaning of parts and limits which are signs of temporality, and we have shown that nothing is like Him, and in that is necessitating making Him like most things. That is sensory or auditory, and if naming by it without the verification we mentioned, the name departs from what is known by it, so knowing by means of intellect and inference is invalidated. Its right is hearing (al-samʿ) from Allah that 'body' (al-jism) is among His names, and it has not been reported from Him nor from anyone whom one is permitted to imitate, so asserting it is not permissible. And if it were permissible by coinage without evidence or intellectual, then asserting body (al-jasad) and person (al-shakhṣ) would be permissible—and all that is rejected by hearing—and asserting every name by which creation is named would be permissible, and that is corrupt. As for the second, it is that 'body' does not have a quiddity (māhiyya) known by it other than affirmation (al-ithbāt), so it would be permissible to assert it if nothing else is meant by it; but no one makes 'body' among the names of affirmation, since accidents and attributes are not named by it despite their possibility of bearing the name of affirmation, therefore asserting it is invalid.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments against applying the term 'body' (jism) to Allah. The first argument is linguistic and revelatory: the term 'jism' implies parts and limits, which are signs of createdness. Since nothing is like Allah, calling Him 'jism' would make Him like most things. Moreover, the term is not used for Allah in revelation nor by those whose imitation is permitted. If one were to coin it without proof, then similar terms like 'jasad' (physical body) and 'shakhṣ' (person) would also be permissible, leading to anthropomorphism. The second argument is conceptual: 'jism' does not have a distinct quiddity other than mere affirmation of existence. If understood abstractly, it might be permissible, but no one uses it that way—accidents and attributes, which are also existent, are never called 'jism'. Thus the term is invalid for Allah.

Key Terms
جسم (jism)
حدث (ḥadath)
سمع (samʿ)
إثبات (ithbāt)
كرية (māhiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'jism' can be used with qualification 'not like bodies'
Correction: Maturidi argues that the term inherently implies composition and limits, which are signs of createdness. Moreover, it is not used in revelation or by the righteous, so it cannot be applied even with qualification.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the term 'jism' not applicable to Allah even if qualified?
A) Because it has no meaning in Arabic
B) Because it implies parts and limits which are signs of createdness, and it is not used in revelation or by the righteous
C) Because Allah is invisible
D) Because it is a synonym for 'shay' (thing)

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi establishes that the term 'body' (jism) cannot be applied to Allah because it implies createdness and is not authorized by revelation, and because it lacks a distinct meaning beyond mere existence.

Why 'Body not like Bodies' is Rejected
Maturidi responds to an objection about applying 'body' to Allah with qualification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the term 'body' (jism) cannot be applied to Allah even with the qualification 'not like bodies', based on the inherent meaning of the terms and the logic of theological affirmation.

Core Idea
You cannot qualify away the core meaning of a term; 'thing' is neutral, 'body' implies parts.

This lesson examines a classic objection: if we call Allah 'thing not like things', why not 'body not like bodies'? Maturidi responds by distinguishing between the scope of terms and the logic of affirmation. His answer clarifies the precision required when speaking about the Divine.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage's first part where the objector asks: if 'thing not like things' is allowed, why not 'body not like bodies'? This sets up the challenge.
The objection sounds plausible but Maturidi shows it is based on a false analogy.
2
First argument: reason does not apply
Notice Maturidi's first point: the reason we call Allah 'thing' (that it applies to all existents) does not hold for 'body'. 'Thing' includes accidents and attributes without implying corporeality, but 'body' implies parts and division.
This highlights the difference in scope and connotation.
3
Second argument: negation does not affirm quiddity
Consider the second point: the negation 'not like things' does not indicate what the thing is (its quiddity). So using that negation to justify 'body' is invalid.
Affirmation and negation serve different logical functions.
4
Third argument and conclusion
Read the analogy with 'human' and the final point: this type of argument is not a valid objection but a presumptuous demand. We do not invent attributes for Allah; we describe Him as He is.
The passage concludes with divine transcendence and a reminder of human limitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إذ قلتم: شيء لا كالأشياء، لمَ لا قلتم: جسم لا كالأجسام؟ قيل له: لأن السبب الذي ألزَمَنا القول بالشيء لم يوجد في الجسم، لذلك لم نقل. وبعد، فإنه لا يخلو فيما يريد إلزامنا من القول بالجسمية من أن يُلزمنا بقولنا بالشيء فوجدنا أكثر الأشياء - وهي الأعراض والصفات من غير لزوم القول فيها بالجسمية - يمنع ذلك. وإن كان يريد إلزامنا بقولنا: لا كالأشياء فليس هو حرف الإثبات ليدل على مائية المثبت، فلا لهذا السؤال. وهو كمن يقول: إذ جاز أن يكون شيئًا لا كالأشياء لمَ لا جاز وجه أن يكون إنسانًا لا كالناس؟ قال الشيخ رحمه الله: فجواب مثله أن يقال: لأنه ليس بجسم فيقال: كالأجسام، وليس هذا النوع بمعارضة إنما هو مُحاكمة؛ ونحن لا نملك إيجاد الصفات للإله حتى نقابل بمثل هذا فيقال لنا: إذ جعلتم ذالِمَ لا جعلتم ذا، بل يتعالى عن الجعل على جهة، بل يوصف بما هو عليه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
If it is said: Since you say 'thing not like things', why do you not say 'body not like bodies'? It is said to him: Because the reason that necessitated our saying 'thing' is not found in 'body', therefore we do not say it. Moreover, what he wants to impose on us from asserting corporeality is not consistent; he imposes on us by our saying 'thing', but we find most things—namely accidents and attributes—without necessitating the assertion of corporeality for them, so that prevents it. And if he intends to impose by our saying 'not like things', that is not a letter of affirmation to indicate the quiddity of the affirmed, so he has no such question. It is like one who says: Since it is permissible for Him to be a thing not like things, why is it not permissible for Him to be a human not like people? The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The answer to such a one is that it is said: Because He is not a body that it may be said 'like bodies', and this type is not an objection but a presumptuous demand; and we do not possess the power to create attributes for the God so that we might oppose with such a thing, like when it is said to us: Since you made Him possessor of knowledge, why did you not make Him possessor of such-and-such? Rather, He is exalted above being made in a certain manner; rather, He is described by what He is upon. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues: if you say Allah is a 'thing not like things', why not say 'body not like bodies'? Maturidi gives several reasons. First, the reason that necessitated calling Allah 'shay' (thing) does not apply to 'jism'. The term 'shay' applies to all existents, including accidents and attributes, without implying corporeality. But 'jism' specifically implies division and parts. Second, the objector cannot use the negation clause 'not like things' to justify 'jism', because that negation does not affirm a quiddity. Third, the objection is analogous to saying: since Allah is a thing, why not a human? The answer is that Allah is not a body, so one cannot say 'like bodies'. This type of argument is not a valid objection but a presumptuous demand. We do not arbitrarily assign attributes; we describe Allah as He has described Himself. The passage ends with a reminder that Allah is transcendent.

Key Terms
شيء (shay)
جسم (jism)
إثبات (ithbāt)
ماهية (māhiyya)
محاكمة (muḥākama)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can apply any term to Allah as long as it is qualified with 'not like creation' (e.g., 'body not like bodies').
Correction: No, because terms carry inherent meanings that cannot be qualified away. 'Body' implies parts and composition, which are incompatible with divine transcendence, regardless of the qualification.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's main argument against saying 'body not like bodies' for Allah?
A) Because 'body' is not mentioned in the Quran
B) Because the term 'body' implies composition and division, unlike 'thing' which applies to all existents
C) Because it would be a contradiction
D) Because it is not Arabic

Key Takeaway
You cannot arbitrarily qualify a term to make it fit for Allah; the term's inherent meaning must be compatible with divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Meaning of 'Thing Not Like Things' and Its Two Types
Maturidi defines the phrase and lists its two components

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi defines 'thing not like things' as negating the quiddity of things, which includes both entity (body) and attribute (accident).

Core Idea
To deny likeness to God, we must negate the very essence of creation, not just similarity.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi unpacks the precise theological meaning of the phrase 'thing not like things.' He shows that it is not merely a denial of similarity but a deeper negation of the quiddity of created things—their fundamental 'whatness.' This distinction is crucial for avoiding both anthropomorphism (tashbih) and divestment (ta'til).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very precise and technical language.
2
Identify the definition and division
The passage begins with the definition of 'thing not like things' as negating quiddity. Then it divides quiddity into two types: entity and attribute. Find these in the text.
The two types correspond to body and accident.
3
Understand the analogy
Maturidi draws an analogy with tashbih and ta'til. He explains that just as removing the meaning of tashbih invalidates statements of anthropomorphism, so removing body and accident from God invalidates the claim that He is a body.
This shows the logical consequence of the definition.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why Maturidi insists on negating quiddity rather than just similarity. This is to protect God's transcendence while affirming His existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كبعض الأشياء»؛ إذ الجسم أحد قسمي الأشياء، وفي ذلك بطلان القول بجسم لا كالأجسام. ولا قوة إلا بالله . {قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : } ثم معنى قولنا : شيء لا كالأشياء» هو إسقاط مائية الأشياء " . وهي نوعان: عين وهو جسم وصفة وهي العرض ، فيجب به إسقاط مائية الأعيان وهي الجسم، والصفات وهي الأعراض، فإذا أزَلْنا ذلك المعنى الذي هو جسم من الأعيان أبطلنا الاسم الذي هو لذلك المعنى، كما إذا أزلنا / معنى التشبيه من الإثبات ونفي [٢٠] التعطيل أبطلنا القول به . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
'Like some things'; because the body is one of the two divisions of things, and in that is the invalidity of the statement 'a body not like bodies.' And there is no power except with Allah. Abu Mansur said: Then the meaning of our statement 'a thing not like things' is the negation of the quiddity (ma'iyya) of things. And it is of two types: an entity ('ayn) which is a body (jism), and an attribute (sifa) which is an accident ('arad). So by it is necessitated the negation of the quiddity of entities, which is the body, and of attributes, which are accidents. So when we remove that meaning which is body from entities, we invalidate the name which is for that meaning, just as when we remove the meaning of tashbih from affirmation and negation of ta'til, we invalidate the statement about it. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage defines the phrase 'thing not like things' used for God. Maturidi explains that it negates the quiddity (ma'iyya) of things, meaning it removes the essential nature that defines created things. Quiddity is of two types: entity (the body itself) and attribute (accidents). So saying 'thing not like things' negates both body and accidents from God. He draws an analogy: just as removing tashbih (anthropomorphism) from affirmation and negation of ta'til (divesting God of attributes) invalidates the statement of tashbih, so does removing body and accidents from God invalidate the claim that He is a body. This is a careful balancing act between affirming God's existence and denying any resemblance to creation.

Correction: The phrase negates the essence of created things, not just similarity.

Key Takeaway

Agreement in name does not imply similarity
Maturidi argues that sharing a name does not entail sharing the same reality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that applying the same term to God and creation does not imply any similarity in essence or attributes.

Core Idea
Shared names do not imply shared realities.

Imam Maturidi addresses a common objection to using the term 'thing' for Allah. If we call both Allah and creation 'things', does that make Allah like other things? He argues that agreement in names alone does not necessitate similarity, and provides linguistic examples to demonstrate this principle. This lesson explores his argument and its implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument and the examples Maturidi provides.
2
Identify the examples
Maturidi gives two examples: 'the unique one of his time' and the pair 'kufr' and 'Islam'. How do these illustrate the point?
Notice that the same word can have different meanings in different contexts.
3
Understand the principle
The core principle is that agreement in names does not necessitate agreement in the underlying reality. Apply this to the question of calling Allah a 'thing'.
This principle defends divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا في القول بالشيء عبارتان : ١ ـ إحداهما أن يُجعل الشيء اسما، والموافقة في الأسماء لا توجب التشابه، لما قد يُستعمل في موضع نفي الموافقة في المعنى، نحو أن يُقال: «فلان واحد عصره وواحد قومه على نفي أن يكون له فيهم نظير أو شبيه من الوجه الذي أريد ، وإن كانوا جميعا في تسمية الواحد شركاء؛ ولو كانت الموافقة في الاسم توجب التشابه لا يحتمل استعماله في موضع إرادة نفي الموافقة. وكذلك نجد قول «كفر) و «إسلام على تحقيق الـ «اسم» لكل واحد منهما، والموافقةُ من حيث القول، ولكن المعنى متناقض ؛ وكذا ذلك في الحركات والأفعال ونحو ذلك.
We have two statements concerning the saying of 'thing': One is that 'thing' is made a name, and agreement in names does not necessitate similarity, because it is sometimes used to negate agreement in meaning, as when one says 'So-and-so is the unique one of his age and the unique one of his people' with the negation that he has any likeness or similar in the aspect intended, even though all are partners in the designation 'one'. And if agreement in name necessitated similarity, it would not be possible to use it to intend negation of agreement. Similarly, we find the saying of 'disbelief' (kufr) and 'Islam' with the realization of the name for each of them, and the agreement is in terms of utterance, but the meaning is contradictory. And likewise is the case in movements, actions, and the like.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents one of two ways to understand the term 'thing' applied to God. Maturidi argues that when we use the same name for God and creation, it does not imply they share the same reality. He gives examples: calling someone 'the unique one of his time' does not mean he is one in the same numerical sense as others. Similarly, 'kufr' and 'Islam' share the word 'name' but have opposite meanings. So applying 'thing' to God does not make Him like other things.

Key Terms
اسم
موافقة
تشابه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Agreement in name between God and creation does not imply any similarity in essence or attributes.

Why God Is Not a Body
Maturidi argues against applying 'body' to God in a real sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'body' cannot be applied to God in a real sense, based on the logical implications of bodiliness.

Core Idea
Bodies imply temporality; God is eternal—thus 'body' cannot apply.

Imam Maturidi here confronts a claim that some might make: that God can be described as a 'body' in a real sense. He builds on earlier discussions about the nature of bodies—their dependency on accidents, their limits—and shows that applying 'body' to God is logically impossible. Let's follow his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Recall the nature of bodies
Read the passage and note the characteristics of bodies that Maturidi mentions: they are 'loci for accidents' and 'subject to limits'.
These are the key properties that will be used in the argument.
2
Follow the conditional reasoning
The passage presents a conditional: if someone insists on calling God a body in a real sense, then we must examine whether the concept of 'body' can be established from every aspect for God.
Pay attention to the structure 'if possible... then... otherwise...'
3
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that saying God is a body is 'impossible and corrupt' because it describes God with something that has evidence of temporality. Reflect on why this is a decisive refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أحب أن يقول في الله بـ «الجسم» على التحقيق مع ما بينا من معاني الأجسام التي محل للأعراض محتملة للنهايات ونحو ذلك يجب أن يُكلَّم في معاني خلق الأجسام المشاهدة إن أمكن تثبيته من كل جهة من جهاته، من حيث تلك الجهة فالقول به في الله محال فاسد، لأنه وصف له بما قام دليل حدثه، وإن كان لا يتهيأ إيجابه فحقه التسمية، إن ثبت قيل به، وإلا لا . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And whoever loves to say of God 'body' in a real sense, given what we have explained of the meanings of bodies which are loci for accidents, subject to limits and so on, then he must be spoken to concerning the meanings of the creation of observed bodies. If it is possible to establish it from every aspect, then from that aspect saying it of God is impossible and corrupt, because it is a description of Him by that which has evidence of temporality. And if it is not possible to affirm it, then the right is to name [Him appropriately]. If it is established, it is said; otherwise not. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage refutes the possibility of ascribing a body (jism) to God. It argues that bodies have characteristics like being subject to accidents and limits, which are signs of temporality (huduth). Since God is eternal, such ascription is impossible. The passage emphasizes logical impossibility.

Key Terms
جسم
عرض
حد
حدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'body' can be applied to God metaphorically or relatively without implying temporality.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even if the term is used in a real sense, it still entails characteristics that are evidence of temporality, making it impossible for the eternal God.

Checkpoint
Why is ascribing a body to God impossible according to this passage?
A) Because God is invisible
B) Because bodies are subject to accidents and temporality
C) Because the Quran denies it
D) Because the intellect cannot conceive it

Key Takeaway
Ascribing a body to God is logically impossible because bodily characteristics entail temporality, contradicting God's eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Two Extreme Views on God and Place
Maturidi enumerates two views on God's location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two views on God's relationship to place and their scriptural proofs and rational arguments.

Core Idea
Literal throne and universal presence both fail to preserve divine transcendence.

Muslim scholars have debated whether God is located on the Throne, everywhere, or neither. Maturidi begins by presenting the two most common views: those who take the Throne verses literally, and those who infer God's omnipresence from other verses. This passage lays out their scriptural evidence and reasoning, inviting us to see the pitfalls of both extremes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first two views
Read the Arabic passage from 'ثم اختلف أهل الإسلام' until 'ففي ذلك إيجاب الحاجة إلى المكان'. Then read the translation to grasp the arguments.
Notice the structure: each view is followed by its scriptural proofs.
2
Identify the first view and its proofs
Find the view that God is on the Throne. Note the verses cited: 20:5, 69:17, 39:75, 40:7 and the practice of raising hands to heaven.
They also argue from 'thumma' to imply temporal sequence.
3
Identify the second view and its proofs
Find the view that God is everywhere. Note the verses: 58:7, 50:16, 56:85, 43:84. Understand their rational objection: limiting God to one place implies deficiency.
They claim any specific location imposes a limit (ḥadd) on God.
4
Compare the two views
Ask yourself how these two views are opposites. One restricts God to a single place, the other diffuses Him everywhere. Both rely on scriptural passages.
Maturidi presents them as problematic; the resolution comes later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة بيان العرش {قال أبو منصور رضي الله [ عنه ]: } ثم اختلف أهل الإسلام في القول بالمكان. فمنهم من زعم أنه يوصف بأنه على العرش مستو؛ والعرش عندهم السرير المحمول بالملائكة المحفوف بهم، بقوله : ويحمل عرش ربك فوقهم يومئذ / ثمانية ، وقوله : وترى الملائكة حافين من حول العرش [الزمر ، ٧٥/٣٩] ، وقوله الذين يحملون العرش ومن حوله ﴾ [المؤمن، ٧/٤٠] الآية . واحتجوا للقول به بقوله: ﴿الرحمن على العرش استوى طه ، ٥/٢٠] ، ويرفع الناس إلى السماء بالدعوات أيديهم وما يأملون من الخيرات. ويقولون: هو صار إليه بعد أن لم يكن، لقوله : ثم استوى على العرش ﴾ [الأعراف، ٥٤/٧] . ومنهم من يقول : هو بكل مكان، بقوله : ما يكون من نجوى ثلاثة إلا هو رابعهم . الآية ، وقوله : ونحن أقرب إليه من حبل الوريد [ق ، ١٦/٥٠]، وقوله: ﴿ونحن أقرب إليه منكم ولكن لا تبصرون ﴾ [الواقعة، ٨٥/٥٦] ، وقوله : وهو الذي في السماء إله وفي الأرض إله ﴾ [الزخرف ، ٨٤/٤٣] ؛ وظنوا أن القول بأنه في مكان دون مكان يُوجب الحد، وكل ذي حدّ مقصر عما هو أعظم منه ، وذلك عيب وآفة ؛ وفي ذلك إيجاب الحاجة إلى المكان، مع ما فيه إيجاب الحد، إذ لا يحتمل أن يكون أعظم من المكان لما هو سُخف في المتعارف أن يختار أحد مكانا لا يسعه ؛ فيصير حد المكان حده ، جل ربنا عن ذلك وتعالى. ومنهم من قال بنفي الوصف بالمكان، وكذلك بالأمكنة كلها إلا على مجاز اللغة بمعنى الحافظ لها والقائم بها . قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : } وجملة ذلك أن إضافة كلية الأشياء إليه، وإضافته عز وجل إليها يخرج مخرج الوصف له بالعُلوّ والرفعة ومخرج التعظيم له والجلال، كقوله:
The issue of clarifying the Throne. {Abu Mansur, may Allah be pleased with him, said:} Then the people of Islam differed concerning the statement about place. Among them are those who asserted that He is described as being upon the Throne, established; and the Throne according to them is the bed carried by the angels surrounding it, by His saying: 'And the angels will be on its sides, and on that Day eight [angels] will bear the Throne of your Lord above them' and His saying: 'And you see the angels surrounding the Throne from all sides' and His saying: 'Those who bear the Throne and those around it' the verse. And they argued for that statement by His saying: 'The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne' and that people raise their hands in supplication towards the sky hoping for good things. And they say: He came to it after not having been, due to His saying: 'Then He established Himself over the Throne.' And among them are those who say: He is in every place, by His saying: 'There is no secret conversation of three but He is the fourth of them' the verse, and His saying: 'And We are nearer to him than the jugular vein,' and His saying: 'And We are nearer to you than your companions, but you do not see,' and His saying: 'And He is the God in the heavens and the God on earth;' and they thought that saying He is in one place rather than another necessitates limitation (al-ḥadd), and everything limited is deficient relative to what is greater than it, and that is a defect and flaw; and in that is necessitating need for place, along with what it contains of necessitating limitation, since it is not plausible that He be greater than place because it is foolish in common understanding that one would choose a place that does not contain Him; so the limit of place would become His limit—our Lord is exalted and greatly elevated above that. And among them are those who said by negating the description of place, and likewise all places, except as a linguistic metaphor meaning the Preserver and Sustainer of them. The Shaykh Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The summary of that is that attribution of all things to Him, and His attribution to them, is in the mode of describing Him with highness and elevation, and in the mode of magnifying Him and [expressing] His majesty, like His saying:
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the differing views among Muslims regarding God's relationship to physical place (al-makān), specifically concerning the Throne (al-ʿarsh). The author (Maturidi) presents three positions. The first view: Some assert that God is literally established on the Throne, which they understand as a physical throne carried by angels, citing verses like 'the Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne' (20:5) and verses about angels bearing the Throne. They also point to the practice of raising hands towards the sky in supplication, and interpret the word 'thumma' (then) in 'thumma istawā' as indicating a temporal sequence, implying God came to the Throne after creation. The second view: Others claim God is everywhere, citing verses about being the fourth of three in secret counsel (58:7), being nearer than the jugular vein (50:16), being God in heaven and earth (43:84). Their reasoning is that restricting God to a specific place would impose limitation (ḥadd) on Him, and what is limited is deficient and in need of that place. This, they argue, is absurd and unworthy of God. The third view: A group completely negates describing God with place, except metaphorically (majāz al-lugha) to mean He is the Preserver and Sustainer of places. The author then gives his own conclusion: All attributive relationships (such as God's attributes being related to things or things being related to God) should be understood as expressions of His transcendence (ʿuluw) and majesty (jallāl), not as literal spatial or physical descriptions. He begins to give an example (perhaps a Quranic verse) but the passage cuts off mid-sentence.

Key Terms
العرش
المكان
الاستواء
الحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Throne is a physical seat and God literally sits on it.
Correction: Maturidi later explains that such descriptions are metaphorical, pointing to divine majesty, not spatial location.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of those who say God is everywhere (bi-kulli makān)?
A) The Throne is too small for God.
B) Placing God in one location imposes limitation and deficiency.
C) God is intangible and cannot be contained.
D) The Quran says 'He is with you wherever you are.'

Key Takeaway
Literal interpretations of God's location lead to either anthropomorphism or pantheism, both of which compromise divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where do you subconsciously imagine God to be when you pray—in the sky above, in the room with you, or nowhere in particular? How does that mental picture influence the intimacy or awe you feel in worship, and are you willing to let go of that picture to fully embrace His transcendence?
?
When you feel spiritually distant, does part of you think God is far away in a physical sense? What would it cost you to accept that He is not bound by place, yet still closer than your own soul?
?
You have likely heard that God is 'with you' and 'above the Throne'—which version gives you more comfort? Can you see how each, if taken literally, actually limits Him? Are you ready to worship a God who is beyond all human categories, even if that feels less immediately tangible?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate

The Metaphorical View and Maturidi's Resolution
Maturidi presents the metaphorical view and his own resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third view (metaphorical interpretation) and Maturidi's conclusion that all attributions to God are modes of exaltation.

Core Idea
God is beyond place; descriptions of place are metaphors for His majesty.

After presenting the two extreme views, Maturidi introduces a third position: that place descriptions of God are purely metaphorical, signifying His preservation and sustaining of creation. He then gives his own summary: all such attributions are to be understood as expressions of divine highness and majesty, not spatial location. This lesson completes the passage and reveals Maturidi's stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the third view and Maturidi's conclusion
Read the Arabic from 'ومنهم من قال بنفي الوصف بالمكان' to the end of the source passage. Read the translation carefully.
Note the term 'مَجاز' (metaphor) and the final statement about 'العُلوّ والرفعة'.
2
Understand the third view
This group denies any literal place for God, except metaphorically as the Preserver and Sustainer of places. They reject both anthropomorphism and pantheism.
This view avoids the pitfalls of the first two.
3
Analyze Maturidi's conclusion
He says that all relative attributions (God to things or things to God) are to be understood as expressions of His highness (ʿuluw) and majesty (jallāl), not spatial relations.
This is the key resolution: transcendence without literal location.
4
Reflect on the incomplete verse citation
The passage ends mid-sentence with 'كقوله:' (like His saying), indicating an example verse that continues on the next page. Consider what verse might follow—likely one that pairs God's greatness with creaturely dependence.
This shows the continuity of Maturidi's argument.

According to Maturidi, how should we understand attributions like 'God is on the Throne'?

?
