Divine Transcendence
intermediate
12 min
Imam Maturidi's Refutation of Anthropomorphic Interpretations of the Throne
Maturidi refutes three interpretations of Allah's relation to the throne.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects three possible interpretations of Allah's relation to the throne (al-arsh) as impossible and contradictory to divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Allah's relation to the throne cannot be understood through spatial categories.

Imam al-Maturidi addresses a critical theological issue: how should we understand Allah's being 'upon the throne' (istawa)? Many anthropomorphists mistakenly attribute bodily or spatial qualities to Allah. Maturidi systematically examines three possible meanings and demonstrates that each leads to absurdities. This lesson walks through his refutation, clarifying the necessity of affirming Allah's absolute transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Problem
Maturidi first argues that if Allah were in a place, He would need that place for support, like bodies and accidents. This is impossible for the Creator.
Notice how the argument begins by establishing that spatial dependency is a property of creation.
2
Identify the Three Interpretations
The passage presents three possibilities for the meaning of 'upon the throne': (1) the throne encompasses Him, (2) He is equal to it, or (3) He surpasses it while it encompasses Him.
These three cover all logical possibilities of spatial relation.
3
Examine the Refutations
For each interpretation, Maturidi shows that it implies limitation, deficiency, or need—all impossible for Allah.
Focus on the reasoning: the first leads to being finite, the second to being less than creation, the third to need.
4
Reflect on the Conclusion
Maturidi concludes that such spatial descriptions are attributes of creatures, not of Allah. Physical elevation does not confer true honor.
This reinforces the principle of tanzih (transcendence).
5
Connect to Broader Aqeedah
Understand that this refutation is a cornerstone of Sunni theology on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن في تحقيق المكان له والوصف له بذاته في كل مكان تمكين الحاجة له إلى ما قراره، على مثل جميع الأجسام والأعراض التي قامت بالأمكنة وفيها تقلبت وقرت؛ على خروج جملتها عن الوصف بالمكان فمن أنشأها وأمسك كليتها لا بمكان يتعالى عن إلى مكان، أو الوصف بما عليه العالم : [من] أن كليته لا في مكان وأنه بجزئياته في المكان. ثم إن الله تعالى لو جعل في مكان لجعل بحق الجزئية من العالم، وذلك أثر النقصان؛ بل لما استقام قيام جميع العالم لا بالأمكنة للجملة، فقيامه على ذلك أحق وأولى. ولا قوة إلا بالله. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم القول بالكون على العرش - وهو موضع - بمعنى كونه بذاته، أو في كل الأمكنة لا يعدو من إحاطة ذلك به، أو الاستواء به، أو مجاوزته عنه وإحاطته به. فإن كان الأول فهو إذا محدود محاط به منقوص عن الخلق؛ إذ هو دونه. ولو جاز الوصف له بذاته بما تحيط به الأمكنة لجاز بما تحيط به الأوقات، فيصير متناهيا بذاته مقصرًا عن خلقه. وإن كان على الوجه الثاني، فلو زيد على الخلق لينقص أيضاً، وفيه ما في الأول. وإن كان على الوجه الثالث فهو الأمر المكروه الدال على الحاجة وعلى التقصير من أن ينشئ ما لا يفضل عنه؛ مع ما يذم ذا من فعل الملوك أن لا يفضل عنهم من المقاعد شيء. وبعد، فإن في ذلك تجزئة بما كان بعضه في ذي أبعاض، وبعضه يفضل عن ذلك، وذلك كله وصف الخلائق، والله يتعالى عن وبعد، فإنه ليس في الارتفاع إلى ما يعلو من المكان للجلوس أو القيام شرف ولا علو ولا وصف بالعظمة والكبرياء، كمن يعلو السطوح أو الجبال، إنه لا يستحق الرفعة على من دونه
After this, indeed, in establishing a place for Him and describing Him by His essence in every place is enabling need for Him to a place of support, like all bodies and accidents that subsist in places and in them they change and settle; while the entirety of them is beyond description by place, so the One who originated them and holds their entirety without place is exalted above [being in] a place, or [above] the description of what the world is: that its entirety is not in a place and that He is with its particulars in place. Then if Allah, exalted, were placed in a place, He would be placed under the law of particularity of the world, and that is an effect of deficiency; rather, since the entire world subsists without place for the whole, His subsistence in that way is more appropriate and more worthy. And there is no power except with Allah. Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Then the statement about being upon the throne—which is a location—meaning His being by His essence, or in every place, cannot go beyond: either the throne encompasses Him, or He is equal to it, or He surpasses it and it encompasses Him. If it is the first, then He is limited, encompassed, deficient relative to creation, since it is above Him. And if it is permissible to describe Him by His essence as being encompassed by places, then it would be permissible to describe Him as being encompassed by times, so He would become finite in essence, falling short of His creation. If it is the second aspect, then if more is added to creation, it would also decrease, and it contains what is in the first. And if it is the third aspect, then it is the disliked matter indicating need and deficiency in that He originates what does not surpass Him; along with what is blamed of the actions of kings that nothing of the seats surpasses them. After this, indeed, in that is a partition into parts, some of Him being in something that has parts, and some of Him exceeding that, and all of that is the description of creatures, and Allah is exalted above... After this, there is no honor, nor elevation, nor description of greatness and pride in rising to a high place for sitting or standing, like one who rises to roofs or mountains; indeed, he does not deserve elevation over those below him.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of anthropomorphic interpretations of Allah's relation to the throne (al-'arsh). Maturidi first argues that if Allah were in a place, He would need that place for support, like bodies and accidents. But the world itself is not confined to a place in its entirety—how could its Creator be? He then presents three possible meanings of the statement that Allah is 'upon the throne': (1) the throne encompasses Him, (2) He is equal to it, or (3) He is beyond it while it encompasses Him. All are impossible. The first implies limitation and deficiency. The second leads to similar problems because if He is equal to the throne, adding to creation would make the creation greater. The third implies need and deficiency, as a king who cannot exceed his seat is faulted. Maturidi concludes that such spatial descriptions are attributes of creatures, not of Allah, and that physical elevation does not confer true honor—which is unique to Allah.

Key Terms
عرش
مكان
إحاطة
استواء
مجاوزة
تحديد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'istawa 'ala al-arsh' means Allah sits on the throne in a bodily sense, occupying space.
Correction: Maturidi shows that any spatial interpretation leads to absurdities; the correct understanding is to affirm the verse without modality (bila kayf) and to negate any anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of interpreting Allah's being on the throne as 'the throne encompasses Him'?
A) It shows His greatness
B) It implies He is limited and deficient
C) It is the correct interpretation
D) It means He is above all

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that any spatial interpretation of Allah's relation to the throne inevitably leads to anthropomorphism and absurdity, underscoring Allah's absolute transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson shows that even kings dislike having their seats not surpass them. What does that reveal about human pride? ?
Maturidi argues that physical elevation does not indicate true honor. Where do you seek honor in your life—through status, position, or recognition? If true honor belongs only to Allah, what practical changes must you make to realign your pursuit of glory?

God is Not in a Place
Maturidi's rational argument against God being in a place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that God cannot be in a place and how this argument reinforces divine transcendence (tanzih).

Core Idea
God is above any place; place implies need and deficiency.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a rational argument to refute the notion that God occupies a place. He argues that place implies need and similarity to created bodies, which is incompatible with God's perfection. This reasoning safeguards divine transcendence (tanzih). Read on to see how he builds his case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style of Maturidi's argument.
2
Identify the claim and premise
Identify the main claim (God is not in a place) and the premise (place implies need and similarity to bodies).
The argument uses analogy and contradiction.
3
Understand the analogy with bodies
Maturidi compares God to bodies and accidents that exist in places. He contrasts their existence with God's exaltation above place.
4
Grasp the consequence of deficiency
If God were in a place, He would be like a part of the world, which implies deficiency (naqsan).
This is a key step in the argument.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Since the entire world as a whole is not confined to a place, it is more fitting that the Creator is not in a place.
This conclusion upholds divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن في تحقيق المكان له والوصف له بذاته في كل مكان تمكين الحاجة له إلى ما قراره، على مثل جميع الأجسام والأعراض التي قامت بالأمكنة وفيها تقلبت وقرت؛ على خروج جملتها عن الوصف بالمكان فمن أنشأها وأمسك كليتها لا بمكان يتعالى عن إلى مكان، أو الوصف بما عليه العالم : [من] أن كليته لا في مكان وأنه بجزئياته في المكان. ثم إن الله تعالى لو جعل في مكان لجعل بحق الجزئية من العالم، وذلك أثر النقصان؛ بل لما استقام قيام جميع العالم لا بالأمكنة للجملة، فقيامه على ذلك أحق وأولى . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Afterwards, investigation of place for Him and describing Him by His essence in every place enables the need for Him to a settled state, like all bodies and accidents that exist in places and in them they change and settle; [this is] on [the basis of] their totality being beyond the description of place. So the One who originated them and sustains their whole, not in a place, is exalted above place, or the description based on what the world is [such] that its whole is not in a place and that He is [present] in its parts in place. Then if God Almighty were placed in a place, He would be placed by the status of a part of the world, and that is an effect of deficiency. Rather, since the entire world stands not through places for the whole, His standing upon that is more fitting and prior. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a rational argument refuting the notion that God occupies any place or location. Maturidi begins by stating that attributing a place to God or describing Him as being in every place would imply that God is in need of a place to settle, which is characteristic of bodies and their accidents. He argues that all physical objects and their properties exist within places and are subject to change. However, the totality of such objects transcends place. The Creator, who brought them into existence and sustains them, cannot be confined to place; He is exalted above it. If God were in a place, He would be like a part of the world, which would imply deficiency. Since the universe as a whole does not exist within a place, it is even more appropriate that the Creator, who sustains it, is not in a place. The conclusion is that God is above any spatial limitation.

Key Terms
مكان (place)
جسم (body)
عرض (accident)
جزئية (part/particularity)
نقصان (deficiency)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God is located in a specific place, such as the Throne, in a physical sense.
Correction: God is not in any place because place implies need and similarity to creation, and that would be a deficiency incompatible with His perfection.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would be the consequence if God were in a place?
A) He would be limited and deficient.
B) He would be everywhere equally.
C) He would be greater in honor.
D) He would be knowable through science.

Key Takeaway
God is not in a place because place implies need and similarity to creation, contradicting His perfect oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
Consider a time when you felt far from Allah because you thought He was 'far away'. After learning that He is not bound by place, how does that change your understanding of His nearness? What concrete step will you take this week to seek His nearness without imagining a place?

Refuting Physical Interpretations of God's Throne
Maturidi refutes three possible meanings of God's istiwa on the Throne.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible physical interpretations of God's 'being on the Throne' and why Maturidi refutes each as incompatible with divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God's 'sitting' on the Throne cannot be physical; all such interpretations imply limitation.

Maturidi, in his Kitab al-Tawhid, tackles a common anthropomorphic misconception about God's relationship to the Throne. He systematically examines every conceivable physical meaning of the phrase and shows each one leads to absurdity. This lesson walks through his logical refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three interpretations
Maturidi lists three possible physical meanings of 'being on the Throne.' Find each one and note how he phrases them.
The three are: encompassment, levelness, and exceeding/encompassing.
3
Follow the refutation for each
For each interpretation, Maturidi gives a logical consequence that contradicts God's perfection. Trace the reasoning for each.
Pay attention to the analogies with kings and creation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
After the three refutations, Maturidi concludes that any physical interpretation implies division and parts, which are attributes of creatures.
This reinforces the doctrine of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول بالكون على العرش - وهو موضع - بمعنى كونه بذاته، أو في كل الأمكنة لا يعدو من إحاطة ذلك به، أو الاستواء به، أو مجاوزته عنه وإحاطته به . فإن كان الأول فهو إذا محدود محاط به منقوص عن الخلق؛ إذ هو دونه. ولو جاز الوصف له بذاته بما تحيط به الأمكنة لجاز بما تحيط به الأوقات، فيصير متناهيا بذاته مقصرًا عن خلقه. وإن كان على الوجه الثاني، فلو زِيدَ على الخلق لينقص أيضاً، وفيه ما في الأول. وإن كان على الوجه الثالث فهو الأمر المكروه الدال على الحاجة وعلى التقصير من أن ينشئ ما لا يفضل عنه؛ مع ما يُذم ذا من فِعْل الملوك أن لا يفضل عنهم من المقاعد شيء . وبعد، فإن في ذلك تجزئة بما كان بعضه في ذي أبعاض، وبعضه يفضل عن ذلك، وذلك كله وصف الخلائق، والله يتعالى عن [؟] .
Then the statement about being upon the Throne – and it is a place – in the sense of His being by His essence, or in all places, does not go beyond [one of] three [possibilities]: either that it (the Throne) encompasses Him, or that He is level with it, or that He exceeds it and encompasses it. If it is the first, then He is limited, encompassed, deficient relative to creation, for He is below it. If the description by His essence of being encompassed by places were permissible, then being encompassed by times would also be permissible, so He would become finite in essence, falling short of His creation. If it is the second aspect, then if an increase were made upon the creation, it would also decrease, and it contains what is in the first. If it is the third aspect, it is the disliked matter indicating need and deficiency in that He originates what does not surpass Him; along with the blameworthy action of kings that no seat surpasses them. Afterwards, that involves division [such that] some part of it is in that which has parts, and some part surpasses that, and all of that is the description of creatures, and God is exalted above [?].
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a logical refutation of three possible interpretations of the statement that God is 'upon the Throne' in a literal sense. Maturidi first presents the three possibilities: (1) the Throne encompasses God, (2) God is level with the Throne, or (3) God exceeds and encompasses the Throne. He then refutes each: (1) If the Throne encompasses God, then God is limited and lower than creation, which is impossible. (2) If God is level with the Throne, then one could imagine an increase in creation that would surpass God, again leading to limitation. (3) If God exceeds the Throne, this implies He is in need of a place and is deficient, as it is blameworthy for a king to sit on a throne that is too large for him. After these refutations, Maturidi states that such interpretations imply division and parts, which are attributes of creatures, and God is exalted above all that.

Key Terms
عرش (Throne)
إحاطة (encompassment)
محدود (limited)
متناه (finite)
تجزئة (division)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God sits on the Throne in a literal physical sense.
Correction: All physical interpretations of istiwa lead to contradictions with God's transcendence and are therefore impossible.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three interpretations of 'being on the Throne' that Maturidi refutes?
A) The Throne encompasses God.
B) God is level with the Throne.
C) God is above the Throne without contact.
D) God exceeds and encompasses the Throne.

Key Takeaway
All physical interpretations of God's being on the Throne imply limitation, division, or need, which are impossible for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah as sitting on a throne in a physical sense, perhaps based on familiar images? What does this lesson reveal about the danger of such a mental image, and how can you actively train your mind to think of Allah's transcendence instead?

Maturidi argues that any physical interpretation of 'istiwa' leads to deficiency or need in Allah. What would change in your worship if you fully internalized that Allah is beyond all such limitations?
?
The passage refutes the idea that God could be 'level' with the Throne, which would imply that an increase in creation decreases God's status. Do you ever act as if Allah needs your worship or as if your good deeds add to His majesty? How can you reorient your obedience to be purely for your own benefit and gratitude, not because Allah is in need?

Refuting Physical Interpretations of Istiwa
Maturidi argues istiwa in the Quran indicates majesty, not physical elevation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's argument that the Quranic mention of istiwa (establishing) upon the Throne signifies divine majesty and not physical elevation.

Core Idea
Istiwa in the Quran points to majesty, not location.

Imam Maturidi tackles a common misinterpretation of the verse describing Allah's 'istiwa' upon the Throne. He insists that the verse's own mention of greatness and majesty precludes a literal physical reading. This passage explains why and reinforces the Throne's exalted created status through a prophetic hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, to get the flow of Maturidi's argument.
The passage is dense; let the meaning settle.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the sentence where Maturidi states it is not permissible to interpret the verse physically.
He says 'فلا يجوز صرف تأويل الآية إليه'.
3
Understand the rationale
Notice how he links the verse to 'عظمة والجلال' (greatness and majesty) and 'تعظيم العرش' (magnification of the Throne).
The Throne is magnified, not Allah.
4
Examine the hadith
Read the hadith about the sun and moon receiving light from the Throne. Ask: How does this support Maturidi's argument?
It shows the Throne's exalted rank.
5
Reflect on the misconception
Consider why someone might think istiwa is physical. How does Maturidi correct that?
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عند استواء الجوهر. فلا يجوز صرف تأويل الآية إليه، فيما فيها ذكر العظمة والجلال . إذ ذكر في قوله تعالى : وإن ربكم الله الذي خلق السموات والأرض الآية؛ فذلك على تعظيم العرش، أي شيء كان من نور أو جوهر لا يبلغه علم الخلق. وقد روى عن نبي الله أنه وصف الشمس : إن جبريل يأتيها بكف من ضوء العرش فيلبسها كما يلبس أحدكم قمیصه كل يوم تطلع . وذكر في القمر كفاً من نور العرش.
Upon the establishment of the substance. It is not permissible to direct the interpretation of the verse to it, in what is in it of mention of greatness and majesty. For He mentioned in His saying 'And your Lord is Allah, who created the heavens and the earth' the verse; that is for the magnification of the Throne, meaning something that was of light or substance that the knowledge of creation does not reach. And it has been narrated from the Prophet of Allah that he described the sun: 'Gabriel brings to it a handful of light from the Throne and clothes it as one of you clothes his shirt every day it rises.' And he mentioned concerning the moon 'a handful of light from the Throne.'
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage refutes a literal physical interpretation of istiwa (establishing) upon the Throne. Maturidi opens by stating that it is not permissible to direct the interpretation of the verse (Quran 7:54) to physical elevation ('istawa' al-jawhar) because the verse itself speaks of 'azama (greatness) and jalāl (majesty). He explains that the mention of the Throne in the verse is for the purpose of ta'ẓīm (magnification) of the Throne, which is a created thing of light or substance that human knowledge cannot fully grasp. To reinforce the majesty of the Throne, he quotes a hadith in which the Prophet describes the sun receiving a handful of light from the Throne each day, and similarly the moon receives a handful of light. This illustrates that the Throne is the source of celestial light, indicating its supreme rank, not that God is physically located upon it.

عرش
تعظيم
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic phrase 'istawa 'ala al-arsh' means Allah physically sits or is located on the Throne.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the verse's mention of greatness and majesty prohibits a physical interpretation; rather, it indicates the Throne's magnificence as a created entity.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that istiwa in the Quran refers to the Throne's majesty, not Allah's physical location, preserving divine transcendence.

Two Reasons for Attributing Istiwa to the Throne
Maturidi gives two reasons why istiwa is specifically linked to the Throne

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two reasons (ta'zim and takhsis) for why istiwa is attributed to the Throne, and how this refutes anthropomorphic interpretations.

Core Idea
The Throne is mentioned not as a location for God, but to magnify His dominion.

Maturidi anticipates a natural question: why is istiwa specifically attributed to the Throne? He provides two interconnected reasons that follow Arab rhetorical practices. Understanding these reasons is crucial to avoid literalist misunderstandings that would compromise divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: Maturidi opens with 'li-wajhayn' (for two reasons) and enumerates them.
2
Identify the two reasons
Find the first reason (ta'zim: magnification) and the second reason (takhsis: specification). Understand how each functions to explain the attribution.
The first reason connects istiwa to God's dominion; the second highlights the Throne's greatness among creation.
3
Examine the analogy of the ruler
Maturidi gives an example from Arab custom: saying a ruler 'established upon a city' implies dominion over all, not just that city. Apply this to the Throne: istiwa on the Throne indicates absolute dominion, not a physical location.
4
Connect to Quranic examples
The passage cites the perfection of religion after the conquest of Mecca (Umm al-Qura) and the sending of messengers to Pharaohs and Mecca. These show that mentioning a specific thing can highlight the greatness of the event, not exclusivity.
This reinforces the second reason that the Throne is specified for its grandeur.
5
Reflect on the overall point
Consider how these reasons preserve God's transcendence. The Throne is a created entity, and attributing istiwa to it is a rhetorical device, not a description of physical occupation.
This connects to the core idea of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإضافة الاستواء إليه لوجهين أحدهما على تعظيمه، بما ذكره على إثر ذكره سلطانه في ربوبيته وخلقه ما ذكر . والثاني على تخصيصه بالذكر بما هو أعظم الخلق وأجلّه ; على المعروف من إضافة الأمور العظيمة إلى أعظم الأشياء ; كما يقال : تم لفلان ملك بلد كذا واستوى على موضع كذا، لا على خصوص ذلك في الحق، ولكن معلوم أن من له ملك ذلك فما دونه أحق. وعلى ذلك قوله تعالى : اليوم أكملت لكم دينكم الآية، بما صارت له أم القرى وأيس الذين كفروا من دينه . وكذا ما ذكر من إرسال الرسل إلى الفراعنة وإلى أم القرى لا يتخصص ذلك ولكن يذكر عظم الأمر، فمثله أمر العرش. وهو كقوله: يقول الله تعالى : وكذلك جعلنا في كل قرية أكابر مجرميها ليمكروا فيها.
Thus the addition of istiwa to it is for two reasons: one is for its magnification, by His mentioning it after His mentioning His dominion in His lordship and His creation what He mentioned. And the second is for specifying it with mention as it is the greatest of creation and the most majestic; according to the known [practice] of attributing great matters to the greatest of things; just as it is said: 'So-and-so completed the rulership of such-and-such a city and established upon such-and-such a place', not upon the particularity of that in reality, but it is known that whoever has the rulership of that, then what is lesser is more appropriate. And upon that is His saying: 'Today I have perfected for you your religion' the verse, because 'Umm al-Qura (Mecca) came under his authority and those who disbelieved despaired of his religion. And likewise what is mentioned of sending messengers to the Pharaohs and to 'Umm al-Qura, that is not exclusive but rather it mentions the greatness of the matter; so similarly is the matter of the Throne. And it is like His saying: Allah says: 'And thus We placed in every town the greatest of its criminals to plot therein.'
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage explains the two reasons (wajhān) why istiwa is specifically attributed to the Throne. The first reason is ta'ẓīm (magnification) of the Throne: God mentions it right after mentioning His dominion (sulṭān) and creation to show its grandeur. The second reason is takhṣīṣ (specification): the Throne is singled out because it is the greatest and most majestic of all created things. This follows a familiar Arab custom of attributing great matters to the greatest thing. Maturidi gives an analogy: when we say 'so-and-so completed the rulership of such-and-such a city and established upon that place,' we do not mean he only rules that city, but rather that he rules it and all lesser territories. Similarly, when God mentions the Throne in the context of istiwa, it is to emphasize His absolute dominion. He supports this with Quranic examples: the perfection of religion after the conquest of Mecca (Umm al-Qura) and the despair of the disbelievers; and the sending of messengers to the Pharaohs and to Mecca. These references are not exclusive but are meant to highlight the greatness of the event. Thus, the Throne is mentioned for emphasis, not as a physical location for God.

Key Terms
إضافة
تعظيم
تخصيص
أم القرى
عرش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That attributing istiwa to the Throne implies God is physically bound to it.
Correction: The attribution is for magnification and specification, not to indicate a physical location. The Throne is a created entity, and istiwa on it does not imply spatial occupation.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason Maturidi gives for attributing istiwa to the Throne?
A) To indicate that God is sitting on it
B) To magnify the Throne by mentioning it after God's dominion
C) To show that the Throne is the only thing God controls
D) To limit istiwa to the Throne exclusively

Key Takeaway
The attribution of istiwa to the Throne is for emphasis on God's absolute dominion, not for limiting Him to a physical location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Throne Negates Spatial Location for Allah
Maturidi interprets the Throne as negating spatial location for Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the mention of the Throne and Quranic verses to negate any spatial location for Allah and affirm His absolute transcendence.

Core Idea
The Throne is mentioned not to place God there, but to show He is beyond all places.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a profound interpretation of the Throne verse. He argues that the mention of the Throne actually negates any spatial attribute for Allah, because it points to the highest place in creation to show that Allah transcends all places entirely. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the Quranic evidence he marshals to demonstrate that Allah is free from spatial location and need.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the passage carefully and locate Maturidi's central claim about the Throne. Notice that he says the Throne is mentioned for negation (nafy) of spatial location, not for affirmation of a place.
The Throne is the highest place, so mentioning it indicates that God is above all places.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
Maturidi cites several Quranic verses to support his interpretation. For each verse, identify what attribute of Allah it highlights (e.g., knowledge, power, authority, divinity).
The verses cover God's nearness as power, His divinity in all places, sovereignty, and subjugation.
3
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that these descriptions show that all attributes belong to Allah's essence (dhāt), not from creation. Reflect on how this reinforces the principle of tanzih (transcendence).
The final sentence glorifies Allah above any likeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحتمل أن يكون على النفي بوصف المكان، إذ هو أعلى الأمكنة عند الخلق ولا تقدّر العقول فوقه شيئًا، فأشار إليه ليُعلم علوه عن الأمكنة وتعاليه عن الحاجة. وعلى ذلك قوله: ما يكون من نجوى ثلاثة إلا هو رابعهم الآية؛ والنجوى ليس من نوع ما يضاف إلى المكان، ولكن يضاف إلى الإسرار. فأخبر بعلوه عن الأمكنة وتعاليه عن أن يخفى عليه شيء؛ ثم بقدرته بقوله: ونحن أقرب إليه من حبل الوريد، أي بالسلطان والقوة، وبألوهيته في البقاع كلها، لأنها أمكنة العبادة، بقوله: وهو الذي في السماء إله وفي الأرض إله، ويملك كل شيء بقوله: له ملك السموات والأرض، ثم بعلوه وجلاله بقوله: وهو القاهر فوق عباده، وقوله: وهو بكل شيء عليم، وقوله: وهو على كل شيء قدير. فجمع في هذه الأحرف ما فرق في تلك ليعلم أنه بكل ما سمي به ووصف كان ذلك له بذاته لا بشيء من خلقه، وكذلك عزّه وشرفه ومجده. جل ثناؤه عن الأشباه، ولا إله غيره.
And it is possible that it is for negation by description of place, since it is the highest of places according to creation, and minds do not conceive anything above it, so He pointed to it to inform of His elevation above places and His transcendence above need. And upon that is His saying: 'There is no secret conversation of three except that He is the fourth of them' the verse; and secret conversation is not of the type that is added to place, but it is added to confidentiality. So He informed of His elevation above places and His transcendence above anything being hidden from Him; then with His power by His saying: 'And We are nearer to him than the jugular vein', meaning by authority and strength; and with His divinity in all regions, because they are places of worship, by His saying: 'And He is who is in the heaven God and in the earth God'; and He owns everything by His saying: 'To Him belongs the kingdom of the heavens and the earth'; then with His elevation and majesty by His saying: 'And He is the subduer above His servants'; and His saying: 'And He is of all things Knowing'; and His saying: 'And He is over all things Powerful.' So He gathered in these letters what He dispersed in those, to make known that He, by everything He is named and described, that is His by essence, not by anything of His creation; and likewise His might, honor, and glory. Exalted is His praise above likenesses, and there is no god but Him.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage offers an additional interpretation: the mention of the Throne is for naffy (negation) of any spatial location for God. Since the Throne is the highest place conceivable by creation (a'lā al-amkina), and minds cannot imagine anything above it, God mentions it to indicate that He is above all places ('uluw 'an al-amkina) and independent of any need (ta'ālī 'an al-ḥāja). Maturidi then supports this with Quranic verses. First, the verse about secret counsel (Quran 58:7): 'There is no secret conversation of three except that He is the fourth of them.' He notes that the term najwā (secret conversation) is not spatial but relates to confidentiality, so God's being with them is not physical nearness but knowledge. This shows His elevation above places and that nothing is hidden from Him. Second, the verse 'We are nearer to him than the jugular vein' (Quran 50:16), which he interprets as nearness by authority and power (sulṭān wa quwwa). Third, verses about His divinity in all places (Quran 43:84) because they are places of worship. Fourth, His sovereignty (Quran 2:107). Fifth, His being 'above' His servants (Quran 6:18) and His knowledge and power (Quran 6:101, 11:4). Maturidi concludes that God gathered in these various descriptions what He scattered in others to show that all attributes belong to His essence (dhāt), not derived from creation. He ends with a glorification: 'Exalted is His praise above likenesses, and there is no god but Him.'

نفي
علو
تعال
سلطان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God being 'above' means a physical direction or location.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'above' and other spatial terms are used to negate place altogether, not to place God in a physical location.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi interpret the verse 'We are nearer to him than the jugular vein'?
A) As physical closeness
B) As nearness by knowledge alone
C) As nearness by authority and power

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the Throne and Quranic verses affirm Allah's transcendence above all spatial attributes, and that His attributes belong to His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Throne is the highest place in creation, yet Allah uses it to show He is beyond all places. Is there any 'throne' in your life—a position, status, or place—that you mistakenly think is where God is? Are you willing to let go of that spatial or material understanding?
?
Maturidi says Allah's attributes belong to His essence, not derived from creation. Do you ever attribute traits to God based on human comparisons? How can you purify your understanding of His might, honor, and glory from any likeness to created things?

God's Transcendence Over Places
Maturidi explains how Quranic verses prove God's transcendence above places.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quranic verses to affirm God's absolute transcendence beyond spatial attributes and location.

Core Idea
God's 'closeness' and 'highness' are metaphors for power and knowledge, not location.

Maturidi confronts a common misunderstanding: when the Quran says God is 'above' or 'near', does that mean He occupies a place? In this passage, he systematically refutes that notion by interpreting key verses in a way that affirms God's absolute transcendence. We'll see how each verse points to an attribute like power or knowledge, never physical proximity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the initial example to the list of verses.
Pay attention to the phrase 'أي بالسلطان والقوة' which is key to understanding 'closeness'.
2
Identify the key Quranic verses cited
List the verses Maturidi references: about secret conversation, jugular vein, God in heaven and earth, kingdom of heavens and earth, and subduer above servants. See how each is introduced.
3
Understand the interpretation of each verse
For each verse, note how Maturidi explains it in a non-spatial sense. For example, 'closeness' of the jugular vein is explained as 'authority and power'.
This is the core of his argument against anthropomorphism.
4
See the conclusion: attributes belong to God's essence
Read the final sentence: 'So He gathered in these letters... that it be known that He, by everything He is named and described, that is His by His essence not by anything of His creation.' This is the logical conclusion.
All attributes are essential, not accidental or spatial.
5
Reflect on the importance for tawheed
Ask yourself: why is it important to deny spatial attributes? How does this protect the concept of God's oneness and transcendence?
This lesson guards against likening God to creation (tashbih).

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument proving God's absolute transcendence above spatial location and physical attributes. The author begins by citing a verse about 'the great ones of its criminals' and 'We command its affluent' to show that God's actions are not bound by place. He explains that God is referred to as the highest of places, not because He occupies a location, but to indicate His highness and lack of need for space. He then lists several Quranic verses to illustrate different attributes: His omnipresence in knowledge (the secret conversation verse), His closeness in power (the jugular vein verse), His divinity in all lands (the God in heaven and earth verse), His absolute ownership (the kingdom verse), and His highness and majesty (the subduer verse). The author concludes that these diverse descriptions all point to God's essence alone, not to any created object or attribute. The passage guards against anthropomorphism by clarifying that terms like 'throne' and 'closeness' are metaphorical expressions of power and knowledge, not spatial realities.

Three Interpretations of al-Istiwa' and the Meaning of al-'Arsh
Maturidi enumerates interpretations of istiwa' and defines 'arsh as dominion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly interpretations of istiwa' and how Imam al-Maturidi interprets 'arsh as God's absolute dominion, rejecting anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
God's throne is His dominion, not a physical seat.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi examines the Quranic terms 'arsh (throne) and istiwa' (establishing). He reports three possible meanings of istiwa'—domination, elevation, and completion—and explains that 'arsh refers to God's sovereignty, not a literal throne. The discussion is a classic example of how kalam theologians defend divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a definition, an enumeration, and a concluding preference.
The passage includes poetry that illustrates the linguistic usage.
2
Identify the three meanings of istiwa'
Find where Maturidi lists three aspects of istiwa'. Write them down and note the Quranic or Arabic evidence he gives for each.
These are istila' (domination), 'uluw (highness), and tamam (completion).
3
Examine the poetic evidence
Read the lines of poetry that use 'arsh to mean kingdom or throne. How do they support Maturidi's interpretation?
The poems refer to the fall of thrones (dynasties), not a divine seat.
4
Understand Maturidi's preferred interpretation
Focus on the final section where Abu Mansur gives his view. What does 'Lord of the Mighty Throne' mean according to him?
He interprets 'arsh as 'great kingdom' (al-mulk al-azim).
5
Reflect on the theological purpose
Ask yourself: why does Maturidi reject a literal physical throne? How does this protect divine transcendence?
This lesson guards against anthropomorphism.

وقال بعضهم: يريد بالعرش الملك، إذ هو اسم ما ارتفع من الأشياء وعلا حتى سمي به السطوح ورؤوس الأشجار. والاستواء قيل فيه بأوجه ثلاثة؛ أحدها الاستيلاء، كما يقال: «استوى فلان على كورة كذا» بمعنى استولى عليها. والثاني العلوّ والارتفاع، كقوله: فإذا استويت أنت ومن معك على الفلك الآية. والثالث التمام، كقوله تعالى: ولما بلغ أشده واستوى. وقد قيل بالقصد؛ إلى ذلك وجه بعض أهل الأدب قوله:

قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم الوجه في ذلك - لو كان على الاستيلاء، والعرش [هو] أنه مستول على جميع خلقه؛ وعلى هذا التأويلُ المحمول [على] غير هذا؛ يدل على الأمرين قوله تعالى: وهو رب العرش العظيم بمعنى الملك العظيم، وفيه إثبات عروش غيره؛ فذلك يحتمل ما يحمل ويحف به الملائكة. والله الموفق.
And some of them said: He intends by the Throne the kingdom, for it is a name for what is elevated from things and high until surfaces and treetops are named by it. And 'istiwa'' has been said to have three aspects: one of them is domination (istila'), as it is said: 'So-and-so istawa over such-and-such a territory' meaning he dominated it. The second is highness and elevation, like His saying: 'When you and those with you are seated (istawaytum) on the ship' the verse. And the third is completion (tamam), like His saying: 'And when he reached his maturity and became firm (istawa).' And it has been said [intending] purpose; towards that some literary scholars directed his saying: [poetry]. And the poet said: 'I thought your throne would not cease nor be changed. When the sons of Marwan, their thrones were overturned and they were harmed as [the tribes of] Iyad and Himyar perished.' And another said: 'When the sons of Marwan, their thrones were overturned...' [apparent repetition]. And al-Nabigha said: 'Thrones they perished after glory and they fell after attaining safety and wealth.' And another said: 'And the warring ones, do you hope for success? After Ibn Jafna and Ibn Mathil, the throne.' The kingdom. 'He directed Himself to the heaven' meaning creation, by analogy with the action of created beings in what follows their action [as] an action that is by purpose, although it is not said of Him purpose. And there is no power except with Allah. And the poet said: and another said: 'I thought your throne would not cease nor be changed.' Abu Mansur—may Allah have mercy on him—said: Then the aspect in that is—if it is upon domination, and the Throne is that He is dominant over all His creation; and upon this interpretation is carried [what is] other than this; He indicates both matters by His saying: 'And He is the Lord of the Mighty Throne' meaning the great kingdom, and in it is proof of thrones other than His; so that admits what is carried and the angels circle around it. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a theological classification and argument about the terms 'arsh (throne) and istiwa' (establishing/being seated). The author begins by reporting that some interpret 'arsh as 'kingdom' (mulk), because the word denotes anything elevated, like roofs and treetops. Then he enumerates three interpretations of istiwa': (1) istila' (domination), as in the Arabic idiom 'he dominated the territory'; (2) 'uluw (highness/elevation), as in the Quranic verse about boarding a ship; and (3) tamam (completion/maturity), as in the verse about a person reaching full strength. He also mentions that some have interpreted it as 'purpose' (qasd), citing a line of poetry as evidence. The author then presents the verse 'He directed Himself to the heaven' as meaning 'creation' (khalq), by analogy, while noting that purpose is not attributed to God in the same way as to creatures. After citing additional poetic examples that use 'arsh to mean kingdom or power (e.g., the lines about Marwan's thrones and al-Nabigha's poem), Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi gives his preferred view: if istiwa' is interpreted as domination (istila'), then 'arsh means that God is dominant over all creation, not a literal seat. He supports this with the verse 'Lord of the Mighty Throne' (rabb al-arsh al-azim), interpreting 'throne' as 'great kingdom' (al-mulk al-azim), and notes that the verse also proves the existence of other thrones (i.e., royal seats) which are carried and surrounded by angels. The passage guards against anthropomorphism by affirming that God's 'throne' and 'istiwa'' are not physical but signify His absolute sovereignty and transcendence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'arsh and istiwa' imply a physical seat or bodily sitting for God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'arsh means kingdom/dominion and istiwa' means domination, highness, or completion—never a literal sitting.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Maturidi interprets 'arsh as God's absolute dominion and istiwa' as His domination, safeguarding divine transcendence from anthropomorphic misconceptions.

Refutation of God Being in a Place or Every Place
Maturidi refutes the claim that God is in a place or every place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that God cannot be in a place or every place, and how this would imply change and contradiction.

Core Idea
God cannot be in a place because that would imply change—impossible for the Eternal.

Imam Maturidi addresses an opponent who claims God is either in a specific place or in every place. He shows that both views are untenable because they imply change in God, which contradicts His eternal nature. Moreover, the claim that 'God is in every place' leads to self-contradictory statements. This lesson walks through his refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once to get the flow of Maturidi's argument. Don't worry about details yet.
Notice how he begins by stating the opponent's position.
2
Identify the main claim and refutation
The opponent claims God is not contained by a place? Maturidi reinterprets this as implying change. Identify where he points out the logical consequences.
3
Understand the analogy about change in place vs. action
Maturidi uses an analogy from the witnessed world: change in place is minor compared to change in action. If change in place is impossible for God, change in action is even more impossible. Follow his reasoning.
This is a key kalam argument.
4
Analyze the contradiction of 'in every place'
Maturidi mocks the opponent's phrase 'He is in every place meaning the world.' He shows it reduces to 'God in every place in the places,' an absurdity. Conclude with the correct view: God knows all places without being in them.
Reflect on how this preserves divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : فما قال بأنه لا يحويه مكان بما كان ولا مكان حق؛ إذ ذلك تغيّر . والقول بالحاجة لا يقوله خصمه ، فتعليق الدفع به خطأ. ثم هو يزعم أنه كان غير خالق ولا رحمن ولا متكلم ثم صار كذلك بعد أن لم يكن ؛ ثبت به التغيّر . بل التغير في المكان أقل ؛ من حيث أن يصير المرء في مكان لم يكن فيه بلا تغيّر نحو أن يتخذ له مكانا يحيط به . ولا يجوز أن يوجد تغير من حيث لا تغيّر في ذات الفاعل في الشاهد. وإذ منع القول بهذا في المكان فهو في الفعل - إذ يكون التغير فيه أشد - أولى . مع ما لا يكون أحد في الشاهد فاعلاً بلا تغيّر يعترضه، وجائز كونه في مكان و هو الذي فيه خلق بلا تغيّر ؛ لذلك كان معنى التغيّر في الفعل أشد . والله الموفق. ثم العجب في قوله : هو في كل مكان بمعنى العالم. والعالم اسم ذاته، وهو بذاته عنده ليس في مكان ولا يحقق الله علمًا ليبلغ المكان الذي قال : هو فيه. تأملوا لتفهموا تناقضه في القول . ثم زعم أنه يحفظه مرة ومرة أنه يفعله ؛ وحفظه وفعله في الأمكنة ليس غير الأمكنة. فصار حاصل قوله: الله في كل مكان في الأمكنة، وذلك خلف من القول، بل هو عالم بالأمكنة كلها قبل كونها وبعد كونها . والله الموفق.
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: What he said that no place contains Him, as it was and no place is true, because that would be change. As for the statement about need, his opponent does not say it, so attaching the refutation to it is wrong. Then he claims that He was not Creator, nor Merciful, nor Speaker, and then became so after not being; change is established by that. Rather, change in place is less, in that a person may come to be in a place where he was not without change, such as taking for himself a place that encompasses him. It is not permissible for change to occur where there is no change in the essence of the agent in the witnessed realm. And since the statement about this in place is prevented, then in action—since change in it is more severe—it is more appropriate. Moreover, no one in the witnessed realm is an agent without change that affects him, and it is possible for him to be in a place and be the one who creates therein without change; therefore the meaning of change in action is more severe. And God is the granter of success. Then amazing is his statement: 'He is in every place meaning the world.' And 'the world' is a name of His essence, and He in His essence according to him is not in a place, and God does not verify a knowledge to reach the place which he said He is in. Reflect to understand the contradiction in his statement. Then he claims that He preserves it once and that He does it another time; and His preservation and action in the places are not other than the places. So the conclusion of his statement becomes: 'God is in every place in the places,' and that is a contradictory statement, but rather He knows all places before their existence and after their existence. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that God is in a place or in every place. Maturidi begins by affirming that saying God is not contained by any place is correct, since containment would imply change in God. He then criticizes the opponent for misunderstanding the argument: the opponent does not actually claim that God needs a place, so refuting that need is misplaced. Next, Maturidi points out that the opponent admits God became Creator, Merciful, and Speaker after not being so, which clearly shows change—a property impossible for God. He then draws an analogy: change in location is less significant than change in essence/action, and since change in location is denied for God, change in action is even more untenable. He further argues that in the observable world, any agent undergoes change when acting, and it is possible for an agent to be in a place while acting without change in that action; but for God, change in action would be more severe. Then Maturidi mocks the opponent's statement that 'God is in every place meaning the world,' pointing out that 'the world' is not a place but a name of God's essence, and that the opponent himself does not place God in a real location. He calls for reflection on the contradiction. Finally, he notes that the opponent says God preserves and creates places, implying God is in those places, which reduces to the absurd statement 'God is in every place in the places.' Maturidi concludes that God is not in places but knows all places before and after their existence.

Key Terms
مكان (place)
تغير (change)
عالم (world)
علم (knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that 'God is everywhere' is a correct expression of omnipresence.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this phrase leads to logical absurdities. The proper belief is that God knows all places but is not contained by any.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that the claim that God is in a place is problematic?
A) Because it implies change in God
B) Because it makes God limited
C) Because it contradicts the Quran
D) Because it denies God's mercy

Key Takeaway
God transcends all spatial categories; He is not contained by any place nor confined to every place, but He knows all places perfectly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you picture God in your mind, do you unconsciously assign Him a location—like 'above the heavens'? This lesson forces you to let go of that image. How does it feel to worship a God who cannot be located anywhere? Does that distance you or draw you nearer?
?
You likely pray facing the qibla, but this lesson says God is not contained in any direction. Does your heart truly believe that, or do you still feel God is 'in front' of you? What would change in your prayer if you deeply internalized that God transcends space?
?
The opponent's mistake was claiming God is in every place. But consider: do you ever act as if God is limited to certain 'holy' places—like the mosque—and less present in your daily life? How does this lesson challenge you to see God's knowledge and presence everywhere, without confining Him?

Ruling on Raising Hands to Sky and Refuting Direction for God
Maturidi explains that directional worship is prescribed, not indicative of God's location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that raising hands to the sky in worship is a prescribed act, not an indication of God's location, and that God transcends all directions.

Core Idea
Direction in worship is for our benefit, not a limit on God.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a common confusion: why do Muslims raise their hands to the sky in supplication? Does it mean God is in that direction? Maturidi clarifies that all directional acts in worship are merely prescribed by God for His servants; they do not imply that God occupies space or is confined to a direction. This teaching protects the principle of tanzih (divine transcendence).

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Notice that Maturidi starts by stating the ruling: raising hands to the sky is an act of worship ('ala al-ibada), not a location indicator.
2
Examine the comparisons
Maturidi draws parallels with other worship acts that involve direction: prostrating towards the ground, facing east or west, facing Mecca during Hajj. Reflect on why each act is performed.
3
Understand the theological conclusion
Maturidi then argues that no direction is nearer to God, and that God is independent of our worship. We worship for our own benefit, not because God needs it. This reinforces tawhid.
4
Identify the warning
Anyone who imagines reaching God in a specific direction does not know God properly. This is a serious caution against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : وأما رفع الأيدي إلى السماء فعلى العبادة؛ والله أن يتعبد عباده بما شاء، ويوجههم إلى حيث شاء. وإن ظَنّ من يظن أن رفع الأبصار إلى السماء لأن ذلك الوجه إنما هو كظن من يزعم أنه إلى جهة أسفل الأرض، بما يضع عليها وجهه متوجها في الصلاة ونحوها ، وكظن من يزعم أنه في شرق الأرض وغربها بما يتوجه إلى ذلك في الصلاة، أو نحو مكة لخروجه إلى الحج ، وفي المشاعر بالسعى فيها كباغي ضالة أو ناحية العدو، ويقصدون قصد من يُغلب على شيء يستنقذ منه ، جلّ الله عن ذلك . ثم الله سبحانه - إذ ليس وجه أقرب إليه من وجه ولا أحق أن يعلمه من وجه ولا في وسع الخلق وجه الوصول إليه من وجه دون وجه، ولا طمع العقول بما هو عالم بذاته - غني عن عبادة خلقه؛ فعبدهم لأنفسهم أن يقوموا بشكر نعمه له المحنة كيف شاء. لا يسبق إلى وهم أحد الوصول إليه في جهة دون جهة إلا من لم يعرف الله حق المعرفة.
The jurist Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: As for raising hands to the sky, it is for worship; and God that He makes His servants worship as He wills, and directs them wherever He wills. And if someone thinks that raising eyes to the sky is because that direction is only like the thought of one who claims that He is towards the direction of the lower earth, by putting his face on it while facing in prayer and the like, and like the thought of one who claims that He is in the east of the earth and its west by facing that in prayer, or towards Mecca for going out to Hajj, and in the sacred places by running in them like a seeker of a lost thing or a direction of the enemy, and they intend the intention of one who overpowers something to rescue from it—God is exalted above that. Then God the Exalted—since there is no direction nearer to Him than another direction, nor more worthy that He be known from a direction, nor within the capacity of creation any direction to reach Him from one direction rather than another, nor is there any aspiration of intellects to what He knows of His essence—He is independent of the worship of His creatures; He made them worship for themselves so that they might perform gratitude for His blessings; the trial is as He wills. No one can preoccupy his mind with reaching Him in one direction rather than another except one who does not know God with true knowledge.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and refutation. Maturidi starts by stating that raising hands to the sky is an act of worship prescribed by God; it does not imply that God is in the sky. He then compares this to other acts of worship that involve facing a direction: prostrating toward the ground (as in prayer), facing east or west, or facing Mecca during Hajj and running in the sacred places. Those who think that God is in that direction are like someone who thinks God is toward the ground or in a cardinal direction. Maturidi says God is exalted above such notions. Then he explains that no direction is nearer to God than another, nor can any direction lead to knowing Him. The intellect cannot aspire to comprehend His essence. God is free from needing worship; He commands worship for the benefit of humans themselves, so they may thank Him for His blessings. The test (of worship) is as He wills. Anyone who imagines reaching God in one direction rather than another does not know God properly.

Key Terms
رفع الأيدي (raising hands)
عبادة (worship)
جهة (direction)
قرب (nearness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that facing a direction in worship (e.g., raising hands to the sky, facing Mecca) means that God is located in that direction.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that these acts are purely prescribed by God for worship; they do not imply that God is confined to a direction or location. God is transcendent and beyond all directions.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of raising hands to the sky according to Maturidi?
A) To indicate that God is above
B) To perform an act of worship
C) To show that God is everywhere
D) To ask for rain

Key Takeaway
Raising hands to the sky is an act of worship ordained by God; it does not mean that God is in the sky or in any direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you raise your hands in dua or face the qibla, do you ever unconsciously imagine God as being 'up there' or 'in that direction'? How would your worship change if you truly believed He is not contained by any direction?
?
Maturidi says worship benefits you, not God. If you neglect prayer or supplication, what does that reveal about your gratitude? Are you using worship as a transaction for God's favor rather than as a path to thankfulness?
?
If no direction leads to knowing God, then what does? How much effort do you invest in seeking knowledge of God through His signs and revelation, versus relying on physical rituals to feel close to Him?

Interpreting Divine Attributes: 'Coming' and 'Going'
Maturidi explains that terms like 'coming' and 'going' cannot imply spatial movement for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi explains that terms like 'coming' and 'going' when attributed to Allah cannot imply spatial movement but must be understood in a manner befitting His transcendence.

Core Idea
God's 'coming' means manifestation, not movement.

This lesson presents Maturidi's method for interpreting anthropomorphic terms in the Quran. He uses the example of 'coming' and 'going' to show that these terms, when ascribed to Allah, cannot carry their literal physical meanings. Through textual evidence and rational analysis, he affirms divine transcendence while preserving the integrity of the revealed language.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the author's own words set the context.
The passage is dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the two categories of 'coming' and 'going'
Notice how Maturidi distinguishes between the physical meanings (transition for bodies, annihilation for falsehood) and the divine meaning (manifestation for truth).
3
Understand the principle of transcendence
Reflect on the conclusion: Allah is exalted above both literal meanings. The second expression reinforces that all states and directions contain His blessings, not His location.
This principle guards against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يفهم منه الانتقال؛ ثم مجيئ الحق يفهم منه الظهور، كقوله: ﴿قل جاء الحق﴾؛ وعلى ذلك ذهاب الباطل بطلانه؛ وذهاب الجسم انتقاله. فهذا محل المجيئ والذهاب في المعروف من الأعراض والأجسام. والله يتعالى عن المعنيين جميعاً، لم يجز أن يفهم من المضاف إليه ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله. للمسألة عبارة أخرى إنه ما من جهة ولا حالة إلا الله على عباده فيها نعم لا تحصى فجعل عليهم بها وفيها عبادات، كما جعل في الجوارح والأموال بما له فيهما من النعم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. على أن السماء هي محل ومهبط الوحي، ومنها أصول بركات الدنيا، فرفع إليها البصر لذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
It is understood from it (the term) transition; then the coming of truth is understood from it (the term) manifestation, as His saying: "Say: The truth has come"; and upon that the going of falsehood is its annihilation; and the going of a body is its transition. So this is the place of 'coming' and 'going' in what is known of accidents and bodies. And Allah is exalted above both meanings; it is not permissible that that be understood from that which is attributed to Him. And there is no power except by Allah. For the issue there is another expression: that there is no direction or state except that Allah has upon His servants therein blessings that cannot be counted, so He placed upon them obligations through them and in them, just as He placed in the limbs and wealth for what He has of blessings in them. And there is no power except by Allah. Moreover, the heaven is the place and the descent of revelation, and from it are the origins of the blessings of this world, so the sight is raised towards it for that reason. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage explains how to interpret anthropomorphic terms (like 'coming' and 'going') when applied to God. It distinguishes between the literal meanings in the physical world and the intended meanings for Allah. It begins by stating that from the term 'coming' one understands transition (movement from place to place), while the 'coming of truth' is understood as manifestation (appearance), not physical movement. Similarly, the 'going of falsehood' means its annihilation, not spatial departure. For physical bodies, 'going' means transition. So in the realm of accidents and bodies, these terms carry their literal meanings. But Allah is exalted above both meanings (i.e., both spatial movement and literal transition), so it is not permissible to understand those literal meanings from what is attributed to Him. Then the passage introduces another expression of the same principle: that in every state and direction, Allah has countless blessings upon His servants, and accordingly He has placed acts of worship in those states, just as He has placed worship in the use of limbs and wealth. Finally, it mentions that the heaven is the place of revelation and the source of worldly blessings, for which reason the sight is raised towards it. This reinforces that physical direction (raising the eyes) does not imply spatial location for God, but rather reverence.

الذهاب
الانتقال
الظهور
الباطل
العرض
الجسم
المضاف إليه
العبادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that terms like 'coming of Allah' imply spatial movement or a change of location.
Correction: The passage shows that when attributed to Allah, 'coming' means manifestation (zuhur) and 'going' means annihiliation (butlan), not physical transition.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'maji' al-haqq' according to the passage?
A) Movement from place
B) Manifestation
C) Annihilation
D) Creation

Key Takeaway
When terms like 'coming' and 'going' are used for Allah in the Quran, they are understood metaphorically—as manifestation and annihilation—not as spatial movement, preserving His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read in the Quran that your Lord 'comes' on the Day of Judgment, what image forms in your mind? Are you willing to surrender that mental image for the sake of Allah's transcendence, or do you cling to a human-like picture? What does that attachment reveal about your tawheed?
?
You have likely thought about God being 'up' in heaven. Does this lesson confront your instinct to locate God? Will you train your heart to think of His nearness without direction, or will you continue to imagine a spatial God? What practical change is needed?

The passage says that in every state Allah has blessings. Are you aware of His blessings even in the states you consider dark or difficult, or do you only see the absence of God? How can you use this insight to reframe your hardships as opportunities for worship?

God's Transcendence Beyond Perceptual Limits
Maturidi explains that God is not perceived through created limits, with examples from shadow, light, darkness, and the moon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God is not subject to the perceptual limits that apply to created things, and that the correct approach is to affirm what is revealed without anthropomorphic interpretation.

Core Idea
Perception means grasping limits; the Limitless cannot be perceived.

Maturidi establishes a fundamental principle about divine transcendence: God is unlike creation in that He has no limits or directions by which He could be perceived. The passage clarifies the meaning of perception (idrak) with vivid examples, then concludes with a methodological rule for discussing vision (ru'ya).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow from general principle to specific examples and final methodology.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the definition of idrak
Maturidi defines idrak as 'standing upon the limits of a thing.' This is key: perception is limited to created things that have boundaries.
Compare with the common misunderstanding that vision equals full comprehension.
3
Reflect on the examples
The examples (shadow, daylight, darkness, moon) illustrate that even visible things may have unknown limits. This shows that vision does not require full perceptual grasp.
The moon example is especially clear: we see it but do not comprehend its size or boundaries.
4
Note the methodological conclusion
Abu Mansur's statement provides the rule: affirm what is revealed, negate anthropomorphic meanings, and do not interpret beyond the text.
This guards against both literal anthropomorphism and unwarranted figurative interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يتغير. على أن لكل شيء حدا يُدرك بسبيله، نحو الطعم واللون والذوق والرائحة وغير ذلك من حدود خاصية الأشياء، جعل الله لكل شيء من ذلك وجها يُدرك به ويُحاط به، حتى العقول والأعراض. فأخبر الله أنه ليس بذي حدود وجهات هي طرق إدراكه بالأسباب الموضوعة لتلك الجهات. وعلى ذلك القول بالرؤية والعلم جميعا. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وبعد، فإن القول بالرؤية يقع على وجوه؛ لا يُعلم حقيقة كل وجه من ذلك إلا بالعلم بذلك الوجه، حتى إذا عُبّر عنه بالرؤية صُرف إلى ذلك، وما لا يُعرف له الوجه - بدون ذكر الرؤية - لزم الوقف في مائيتها على تحقيقها. وأما الإدراك إنما هو معنى الوقوف على حدود الشيء. ألا ترى أن الظل في التحقيق يُرى لكنه لا يدرك إلا بالشمس، وإلا كان مرئيا على ما يُرى لوقت نسخ الشمس؛ ولكن لا يُدرك بالرؤية إلا بما يتبين له الحد. وكذلك ضوء النهار يُرى، لكن حده لا يُعرف بذاته. وكذلك الظلمة لأن طرفها لا يرى فيُدرك ويُحاط به. وبالحدود يُدرك الشيء وإن كان يرى لا بها؛ ولذلك ضرب المثل بالقمر، أنه لا يُعرف حده ولا سعته ليوقف ويحاط به ويُرى بيقين. ولا قوة إلا بالله. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: والأصل فيه القول بذلك على قدر ما جاء، ونفي كل معنى من معاني الخلق، ولا يفسر، لما لم يجئ في ذلك تفسير. والله الموفق.
It does not change. According to that, for everything there is a limit by which it is perceived, such as taste, color, flavor, smell, and other limits of the property of things. God made for everything of that a face by which it is perceived and encompassed, even intellects and accidents. So He informed that He is not possessed of limits and directions that are ways of perceiving Him by means of the causes placed for those directions. And upon that is the statement concerning vision (ru'ya) and knowledge (ilm) all together. And there is no power except by God. Afterwards, the statement concerning vision falls upon aspects; the reality of each aspect is not known except by knowledge of that aspect, so that when it is expressed by vision it is directed to that, and what is not known for its aspect - without mention of vision - necessitates stopping regarding its reality until it is verified. As for perception (idrak), it is only the meaning of standing upon the limits of a thing. Do you not see that the shadow in reality is seen but is not perceived except by the sun, otherwise it would be visible as it is seen at the time of the sun's removal; but it is not perceived by vision except by what makes clear its limit. Likewise, the light of day is seen, but its limit is not known by itself. Likewise, darkness because its edge is not seen so it is perceived and encompassed. And by limits a thing is perceived even if it is seen without them; and for this reason the example of the moon was given: its limit nor its extent are known so that one may stop and encompass it, and it is seen with certainty. And there is no power except by God. Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: The principle regarding it is to speak about it to the extent that it came, negating every meaning from the meanings of creation, and not to interpret it because no interpretation came regarding that. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage explains that God is not subject to the perceptual limits that apply to created things. It begins by stating that every created thing has a limit (hadd) through which it is perceived, like taste, color, and smell. God, however, has no such limits or directions that would make Him perceptible by those means. The discussion of vision (ru'ya) and knowledge should follow this principle. The passage then clarifies that perception (idrak) means 'standing upon the limits of a thing.' Examples follow: a shadow is seen but its limit is only known by the sun; daylight is seen but its limit is not known inherently; darkness is seen but its extent is not perceived because its edge is not seen. The moon is cited as an example of something that is seen with certainty but whose limits and extent are not comprehended. The passage concludes with a methodological statement from Abu Mansur al-Maturidi: the correct approach is to affirm what has been revealed concerning vision, to negate any anthropomorphic meanings, and not to interpret further in the absence of textual interpretation.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الإدراك
الحد
القول بالرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That vision of God implies fully comprehending His essence.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between seeing (ru'ya) and perceiving (idrak). Perception requires grasping limits, but God has no limits, so vision of God does not entail comprehension.

Checkpoint

A) To see something clearly
B) To stand upon the limits of a thing
C) To have knowledge of something
D) To exist in space

Key Takeaway
God's essence is beyond perceptual limits, and we affirm vision without anthropomorphism, following the revealed text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Maturidi says we must affirm vision without anthropomorphism. Is there an area in your belief where you have tried to picture God in ways that might be limiting? How can you practice stopping at the revealed text without seeking explanations for what is not given?

intermediate
