Refuting Physical Conditions for Vision of God
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's condition-based argument for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that vision of God requires the same physical conditions as vision of created beings.

Core Idea
God's vision transcends the physical conditions needed for created sight.

Al-Ka'bi argued that since knowledge of the unseen follows the same principles as knowledge of the seen, vision of God must also require conditions like distance, facing, and air connection. Maturidi counters this by pointing out that many created beings are seen without full comprehension, that vision differs due to veils, and that things like shadows are seen without any of those conditions. This passage dismantles the analogy between divine and human vision.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Ka'bi's claim
Read the first part of the passage where al-Ka'bi argues that vision of God requires the same conditions as vision of visible objects.
Notice the conditions he lists: separation, distance, facing, etc.
2
First objection: other essences
Maturidi's first refutation: al-Ka'bi wrongly measures God by his own essence. Yet angels, jinn, and small insects are seen without being fully comprehended.
3
Second objection: veils
In the witnessed world, two sights differ due to veils, so uniform conditions are invalid.
4
Third and fourth objections
Maturidi gives two more arguments: shadow, darkness, and light are seen without those conditions; and all conditions can be present without vision.
The fourth point is cut off but the idea is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج الكعبي بأنه الإدراك . وقد بينا ذلك . ثم زعم أن العلم بالغائب إذ لم يخرج عن الوجوه التي بها يُعلم، فكذلك لا يُرى إلا بالوجوه التي بها يُرى من المباينة للمرئي - ولما حَلَّ فيه المرئي ـ بالمسافة، والمقابلة، واتصال الهواء، والصغر وعدم الصغر، والبعد، ولو جازت الرؤية بخلاف هذا الجاز العلم به. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : وقد أخطأ في هذا الفصل بوجوه. أحدها أنه قدر برؤية جوهره ، وقد علم أن غير جوهره جواهر يُرون من الوجه الذي لا يقدر على الإحاطة بجوهره فضلاً عن إدراك بصره، نحو الملائكة والجن وغيرهم، مما يروننا من حيث لا نراهم والجثة الصغيرة نحو البق والبعوض ونحو ذلك مما يُرى، لما لو توهم مثل ذلك البصرُ لَما احتمل الإدراك . ويرى الملك الذي يكتب جميع أفعالنا ويسمع جميع أقوالنا؛ على ما إذا أردنا تقدير ذلك بما عليه جبلنا للزم إنكار ذلك كله، وذلك عظيم. وكذلك ما ذكر من نطق الجلود والجوارح وغيرها مما لو امتحن بمثلها أمر الشاهد لوجد عظيما. وبعد، فإنه في الشاهد يفصل بين البصرين في الرؤية والتمييز على قدر تفاوتهما، بما اعتراهما من الحجب مما لو قابل أحدهما بحال الآخر على حالته وجده مُستنكرًا؛ وإذا كان كذلك بطل التقدير بالذي ذكر. والله الموفق. وأيضاً إنه في الشاهد - بكل أسباب العلم - لا يعلم غير العرض والجسم؛ ثم جاء من العلم بالغائب خارجا منه، فمثله الرؤية . والله أعلم. والثالث ما بينا من رؤية الظل والظلمة والنور من غير شيء من تلك الوجوه. والرابع أنه قد يجوز وجود تلك المعاني كلها مع عدم الرؤية، إما بحجب أو بخاصية جوهر؛ فجاز تحقيق الرؤية على نفي تلك المعاني؛ نحو ما أجيب القائل بالجسم عند
Then al-Ka'bi argued that it is perception. And we have explained that. Then he claimed that knowledge of the unseen, since it does not depart from the aspects by which it is known, likewise it is not seen except by the aspects by which it is seen: separation from the visible, and what the visible inheres in—by distance, facing, connection of air, smallness and absence of smallness, and distance—and if vision were permissible contrary to this, then knowledge of it would be permissible. Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: He has erred in this section in several respects. One of them is that he measured [it] by the vision of his own essence, and it is known that other than his essence are essences that are seen in a manner by which they are not able to encompass his essence, let alone the perception of his sight, such as the angels, the jinn, and others that see us from where we do not see them, and the small body like the flea and the mosquito and the like that are seen, because if the sight imagined such, it would not bear perception. And the angel who records all our deeds and hears all our words is seen, in such a way that if we attempted to measure that by what we are naturally composed, it would necessitate denying all that, and that is great. Likewise what was mentioned of the speaking of skins and limbs and other things, which if one tested the matter of the witnessed world with their like, he would find it great. Afterwards, in the witnessed world, there is distinction between two sights in vision and discernment according to their disparity, by what afflicts them of veils, such that if one compared one to the condition of the other in his state, he would find it objectionable; and if it is so, the estimation by what he mentioned is invalid. And God is the grantor of success. Also, in the witnessed world—with all causes of knowledge—nothing is known except accident and body; then knowledge of the unseen came from outside that, and likewise vision. And God knows best. And the third is what we have explained regarding the vision of shadow, darkness, and light without any of those aspects. And the fourth is that it is possible for all those meanings to exist without vision, either due to a veil or by a property of essence; so it is possible to realize vision while negating those meanings, like the answer given to the one who posits a body when...
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazili al-Ka'bi's argument that vision (ru'ya) is equivalent to perception (idrak), requiring specific physical conditions. Maturidi first states al-Ka'bi's claim: knowledge of the unseen does not depart from the aspects by which it is known, so similarly vision of God would require conditions like distance, facing, air connection, etc. Maturidi then refutes this with multiple points. First, al-Ka'bi wrongly measures God by his own human essence; yet there are created essences (angels, jinn, small insects) that are seen but not fully comprehended, and even the recording angel is seen though we cannot measure it by our own nature. Second, in the witnessed world, two sights differ due to veils, so the analogy of uniform conditions fails. Third, the example of shadow, darkness, and light shows that these are seen without any of the listed conditions. Fourth, those conditions can all be present yet vision still not occur (due to a veil or a property of the essence), so it is possible for vision to occur without them, just as the answer given to the corporealist (who posits a body) shows. The passage ends abruptly while explaining the fourth point.

Key Terms
الإدراك
الرؤية
المباينة
المسافة
المقابلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That vision of God requires physical conditions like distance, facing, and air connection, as in human sight.
Correction: Maturidi shows that many created beings are seen without full comprehension, veils affect vision, shadows are seen without conditions, and conditions can be present without vision; therefore, God's vision is not bound by such physical conditions.

Checkpoint
What does the example of shadow, light, and darkness demonstrate according to Maturidi?
A) They are not seen at all
B) They are seen but without requiring the physical conditions of vision
C) They are only seen when those conditions are present
D) They are not real entities

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's four objections dismantle the analogy between human vision and vision of God, showing that physical conditions are irrelevant for divine vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have been limiting your perception of God to what you can comprehend—based on your own experience or conditions—what has that prevented you from truly seeing Him in your life? Will you now ask Him to show Himself to you beyond your limited conditions?
?
The lesson shows that even created beings see without being fully understood. Are you content to only see God according to your own capacity, or are you willing to accept that His vision transcends your understanding? How will you seek a deeper vision of Him today?
?
Maturidi points out that conditions for vision can exist without actual vision due to veils. What veils have you placed between yourself and God—your own expectations, assumptions, or desires—that prevent you from experiencing His presence? What will you remove today?

The Wisdom Behind Musa's Request for Ru'ya
Maturidi explains the purpose of Musa's request and rebuts an objection about the mountain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that Musa's request for vision was not a sin but a trial to elevate his rank and to teach the creation about ru'ya.

Core Idea
Musa's request was a trial, not disobedience.

Maturidi addresses a common objection that Musa's request for ru'ya was a sin. He explains that it was actually a trial from Allah, intended to raise Musa's rank and to teach the people about the permissibility of ru'ya in the afterlife. He also responds to an argument about the mountain and the sign.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the purpose
Read the first part of the Arabic passage (up to 'وبالله التوفيق') and its translation. Notice that Maturidi states the request was for conveying the message and as a trial.
2
Analyze the argument about the mountain
Then read the part where Maturidi responds to an opponent who says only the mountain was seen. Pay attention to how he distinguishes between the sign and the vision.
The opponent argues that no rational being was seen, but Maturidi shifts the focus to the mountain as a sign.
3
Reflect on Maturidi's reasoning
Consider why Maturidi emphasizes that the sign is the crushing of the mountain, not the seeing. How does this defend the permissibility of ru'ya in the afterlife?
Connect to the learning objective: the request was for teaching, not for actual vision in this world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك لا يكون بغير الممتحن؛ إذ هو تبليغ الرسالة والدعاء إلى العبادة وهي محنة. بل سأل الرؤية ليجل به قدره وليعرف عظيم محله عند الله، أو أن يكون الله أمره به ليعلم الخلق جواز ذلك. وبالله التوفيق. ثم استدل بأنه لم ير من يعقل، إنما أرى الجبل، والجبل لا يعقل ليعلمه وليراه. فيقال له: ولو كانت آية فالجبل لا يراها ولا يعقل. وإذا كان كذلك فالآية إذا صار اندكاك الجبل لا أن أراه الآية ليندك بها. وفي هذا أنه قد أرى موسى الآية وهو الجبل؛ والله تعالى يقول: لن تراني، وحمله على الآية، وقد رآها. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم سأل نفسه عن معنى توبته، ولا يسأل عنه، فزعم أنه لوجهين. أحدهما أنه علم بما أراه من الأدلة أن ذلك صغيرة فتاب عنها. والثاني على العادة في الخلق من تجديدها عند الأهوال بلا حدوث ذنب. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: ولو كان صغيرة لكان أولى من تركه إلى أن يعلم بالأدلة. وهي في الإعلام في غير حال الإغماء أحق منها في حال الإغماء. والثاني يصح ذلك عند معاينته الهول لا عند سكونه وإبداله بالأمن والإفاقة؛ وذلك وقت معاينته عصاه يهتز فولى مديرا أحق. والله الموفق.
And that is not possible without the test; for it is the conveying of the message and the call to worship, and it is a trial. Rather, he asked for the vision to elevate his rank and to know the greatness of his station with Allah, or that Allah commanded him to do so to let the creation know the permissibility of that. And by Allah is success. Then he argued that no rational being was seen, only the mountain was shown, and the mountain does not reason to know it and see it. So it is said to him: Even if it were a sign, the mountain does not see it nor reason. And if that is so, then the sign is when the mountain became crushed, not that I see the sign to be crushed by it. And in this, that he indeed showed Musa the sign, and it is the mountain; and Allah the Exalted says: 'You will not see Me', and he took it as the sign, and he saw it. And there is no power except with Allah. Then he questioned himself about the meaning of his repentance, and he is not asked about it, so he claimed it was for two reasons. One: that he knew from the evidence shown to him that it was a minor sin, so he repented from it. The second: according to the habit among creation of renewing it at terrors without the occurrence of sin. Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: If it were a minor sin, it would have been more appropriate to leave it until it was known by the evidence. And that [information] about informing in a state other than unconsciousness is more fitting than in a state of unconsciousness. And the second is valid at the time of witnessing the terror, not at its calming and its replacement with security and consciousness; and that is at the time of his witnessing his staff quivering, so he turned away fleeing; it is more appropriate. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the story of Musa (Moses) asking to see Allah. Maturidi first states that the request was for the purpose of conveying the message and calling to worship, not out of doubt or disobedience. It was a trial. Then he considers an objection: the opponent argues that no rational being was seen, only the mountain, and the mountain is not rational to know and see. Maturidi replies that even if it were a sign, the mountain does not see it nor reason. The sign is the crushing of the mountain, not that Musa saw the sign to be crushed. He then notes that Allah showed Musa the sign, which is the mountain, and said 'You will not see Me', but Musa took it as the sign and saw it. Then he discusses Musa's repentance, giving two interpretations: either it was a minor sin he repented from, or it was a habitual renewal of repentance without sin. Maturidi comments that if it were a sin, informing of impossibility would be more appropriate before the evidence. He concludes that the repentance occurred during the awe of the miracle, not after calm.

Key Terms
الرؤية (al-ru'ya)
الممتحن (al-mumtahan)
اندكاك الجبل (indikak al-jabal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Musa's request for ru'ya was a sin or act of disobedience.
Correction: Maturidi explains it was a trial and a means to elevate his rank, not a sin.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is one reason Musa asked for ru'ya?
A) Because he doubted Allah's existence
B) To elevate his rank and show the creation the permissibility of ru'ya
C) Because he was forced by the people
D) To test Allah's power

Key Takeaway
Musa's request for ru'ya was a divinely ordained trial to teach the creation about the permissibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife, not a sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When Musa, a prophet of immense rank, was tested with a request that could be misunderstood as disobedience, do you view your own trials as opportunities for elevation or as failures? What would change if you saw every difficulty as a divine test to raise your station?
?
If even Musa's request was a teaching moment for the creation, are you willing to submit your deepest questions and doubts to Allah's wisdom, trusting that He may be teaching others through you? Will you silence your complaints and let your patience become a sign for those around you?
?
The mountain crumbled at the manifestation of Allah's glory, while Musa survived. Do you think your ego could survive if Allah revealed His true majesty to you today? What in your life needs to be crushed first so that you can truly see Him?

The Repentance of Musa: Tawba Without Sin?
Maturidi discusses two interpretations of Musa's repentance and Abu Mansur's critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two interpretations of Musa's repentance: as repentance from a minor sin or as a habitual renewal without sin, and Abu Mansur's critique.

Core Idea
Musa's repentance may not have been for a sin.

After the incident of the mountain, Musa repented. Maturidi explores two possible meanings: either it was repentance from a minor sin that he learned through evidence, or it was a renewal of repentance customary among prophets during awe-inspiring events. Abu Mansur adds a critical comment about the timing and nature of such repentance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
Read the passage about Musa's repentance (from 'ثم سأل نفسه عن معنى توبته' to the end). Notice Maturidi's two reasons: repentance from a minor sin or habitual renewal without sin.
2
Examine Abu Mansur's objection
Read Abu Mansur's comment: if it were a minor sin, it would be more appropriate to leave it until it was known by evidence. Consider the logic.
The objection questions the consistency of the first interpretation.
3
Consider the timing of repentance
Maturidi's second interpretation suggests repentance during the terror, not after calm. Reflect on why this fits prophetic behavior.
Connect to the story of Musa and the staff quivering (Quran 20:21).

?

Refuting the Anthropomorphist Analogy: The Quranic Argument
Maturidi refutes anthropomorphist analogy using Quranic transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the anthropomorphist analogy and his use of Quranic verse to establish divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphists argue from analogy; Maturidi shows their analogy is incomplete.

In this passage from Kitab al-Tawhid, Imam Maturidi confronts the anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha) who claim that since all agents in the visible world are bodies, the unseen Agent (Allah) must also be a body. He dismantles their reasoning by exposing its incompleteness and then upholds Quranic transcendence. Let's follow his refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the anthropomorphist claim
The opponents assert that every agent and knower in the visible world is a body, so by analogy the unseen Agent must also be a body. This is the core of their argument.
Anthropomorphists rely on a simple analogy from the visible to the invisible.
2
Identify Maturidi's first criticism
Maturidi notes that the opponent only mentioned the meaning of seeing a body, not the meaning of seeing a non-body. Thus the analogy is incomplete and cannot serve as a proof.
The argument is based on a partial observation, not a universal principle.
3
Examine the second criticism
The opponent negates vision by fineness (diqqa) and distance (bu'd), but these conditions are irrelevant to Allah. Maturidi points out these are removed from Allah, so the negation does not apply.
The conditions of fineness and distance are specific to physical bodies.
4
Explore the Quranic argument
Maturidi cites the verse 'No vision can grasp Him, but He grasps all vision' as a permanent attribute of Allah. He argues that just as the attribute of creation and power never cease, this attribute of transcendence also never ceases.
The verse is used to affirm that Allah transcends bodily vision, not to deny vision in the afterlife.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how Maturidi's argument preserves divine transcendence without denying other attributes like vision. It shows that Quranic proofs are consistent and comprehensive.
This refutation is a model for using scripture and reason together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن من تأمل الذي ذكر عرفه أنه مشتهي النحلة؛ لأنه لم يذكر المعنى الذي له يجب أن تكون الرؤية بتلك الشرائط، إنما أخبر أنه كذلك وجد، وهو قول المشبهة، أنه وجد كل فاعل في الشاهد جسما، وكذا كل عالم، فيجب مثله في الغائب. ثم ذكر معنى رؤية الجسم ولم يذكر معنى رؤية غير الجسم حتى يكون له دليلا. وبعد، فإنه نفي الرؤية بالدقة والبعد، وهما زائلان عن الله سبحانه. ثم احتج بامتداح الله بقوله: ولا تدركه الأبصار وهو يدرك الأبصار، وقال: لا يجوز أن يزول. فمثله عليه في قوله: خالق كل شيء، وقوله: وهو على كل شيء قدير، فلا يجوز أن يزول. ثم قد وصف الله بالرؤية
Then the principle is that whoever reflects on what was mentioned knows that he is a follower of whim; because he did not mention the meaning by which vision must be with those conditions, he only reported that he found it so, and it is the saying of the anthropomorphists, that he found every agent in the witnessed world to be a body, and likewise every knower, so the like must be in the unseen. Then he mentioned the meaning of seeing a body and did not mention the meaning of seeing a non-body, so that it would be a proof for him. Afterward, he negated vision by fineness and distance, and both are removed from Allah the Exalted. Then he argued by Allah's praise in His saying: 'No vision can grasp Him, but He grasps all vision', and said: it is not permissible for it to cease. So the same applies to Him in His saying: 'Creator of everything', and His saying: 'And He is over all things competent', so it is not permissible for it to cease. Then He described Allah with vision
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the anthropomorphist (al-mushabbiha) position. Maturidi says: 'Then the principle is that whoever reflects on what has been mentioned knows that he (the opponent) is a follower of whim (mushtahi al-nahla) because he did not mention the meaning by which vision must be with those conditions; he only reported that he found it so.' The opponent claims that every agent in the visible world is a body, and every knower is a body, so the same must apply to the invisible. But he only mentioned the meaning of seeing a body, not the meaning of seeing a non-body, so his argument fails. Then Maturidi says: 'Afterwards, he negated vision by fineness and distance, both of which are removed from Allah.' Then he argues using the verse 'No vision can grasp Him, but He grasps all vision' and says it is not permissible for that to cease. He compares it to the verses 'Creator of everything' and 'He is over all things competent' which also cannot cease. Then he says 'Then He described Allah with vision' (the sentence is incomplete, likely continuing to the next page).

Key Terms
المشبهة (al-mushabbiha)
الدقة (al-diqqa)
البعد (al-bu'd)
ولا تدركه الأبصار (wa la tudrikuhu al-absar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's main criticism of the anthropomorphist argument?
A) It uses weak hadith
B) It only mentions seeing a body, not seeing a non-body, thus incomplete
C) It relies on reason alone
D) It denies the attributes of Allah

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes anthropomorphism by exposing the incompleteness of their analogy and affirming divine transcendence through the Quranic verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does Maturidi’s exposure of the anthropomorphists’ weak analogy make you question those mental images? What concrete step can you take today to purify your concept of Allah from any trace of physicality?
?
The anthropomorphists insisted on their flawed analogy because they trusted their own reasoning over divine revelation. What is one assumption you need to abandon right now?
?
Maturidi argues that the attribute of being beyond vision is as eternal and unchanging as Allah’s power and creatorship. Does your heart truly accept that Allah cannot be seen in this world, or is there a secret wish to perceive Him physically? How would your relationship with Allah change if you fully internalized that He is beyond your senses, yet closer to you than your jugular vein?

The Vision of God Without Modality
Maturidi answers the question of how God is seen, negating all modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the vision of Allah is without modality and how Maturidi negates all physical attributes in describing the vision.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is real but without any physical form, place, or direction.

This passage deals with a common question: if believers will see Allah in the afterlife, how can that be? Imam Maturidi answers by clarifying that the vision is real but completely unlike any physical sight. He negates all modalities that apply to created beings, establishing that Allah's vision transcends any how.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the objection-and-answer format: 'If it is said... it is said.'
2
Identify the objection and response
The questioner asks 'How is He seen?' and the answer is 'Without how.' Understand that the negation of 'how' is the core response.
The word 'kayf' (how) is the root of modality.
3
Examine the list of negated attributes
The passage lists many physical descriptions that are negated: standing, sitting, light, darkness, etc. Notice that all these imply form or limitation.
This list shows that the vision of Allah is free from all created characteristics.
4
Reflect on the reason for negation
The passage states that modality belongs only to beings with form (sura), and Allah is transcendent (ta'ala) above form. This is why the vision is without how.
Transcendence is the key principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : كيف يُرى ؟ قيل: بلا كيف؛ إذ الكيفية تكون لذى صورة؛ بل يُرى بلا وصف قيام وقعود، واتكاء وتعلق واتصال وانفصال، ومقابلة ومدابرة ، وقصير وطويل، ونور وظلمة، وساكن ومتحرك، ومماس ومباين، وخارج وداخل ، ولا معنى يأخذه الوهم أو يقدره العقل، لتعاليه عن ذلك.
If it is said: How is He seen? It is said: Without how; for modality belongs to one possessing form; rather, He is seen without description of standing or sitting, leaning or attachment, connection or separation, facing or turning away, shortness or length, light or darkness, stationary or moving, touching or separating, outside or inside, and no meaning that imagination takes or the mind estimates, due to His transcendence above that.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the vision of God (ru'ya). The questioner asks: 'How is God seen?' The answer is: 'Without how' (bila kayf), establishing that the vision of God does not involve modality because modality applies only to beings with form (sura). The answer proceeds to negate a comprehensive list of attributes that would imply modality: standing, sitting, leaning, attachment, connection, separation, facing, turning, shortness, length, light, darkness, movement, stillness, touching, separation, outside, inside, and any meaning that imagination (wahm) or intellect ('aql) could conceive. All these are negated because God is transcendent (ta'ala) above them.

Key Terms
رؤية
كيف
كيفية
صورة
تعالٍ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that seeing God implies physical conditions or that the vision is metaphorical.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the vision is real but without modality because Allah has no form; it is not a physical sight.

Checkpoint

A) Because modality belongs only to beings with form, and God has no form.
B) Because the vision of God is metaphorical and not real.
C) Because God cannot be seen at all.
D) Because seeing God requires special spiritual vision.

Key Takeaway
The vision of Allah is real but entirely free from any physical modality because Allah transcends all forms and limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Absolute Name Does Not Imply Anthropomorphism
Maturidi argues that absolute name does not imply anthropomorphism but distinguishes opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's argument that using the same name for God and creation does not imply anthropomorphism, because names serve to distinguish opposites, not to indicate shared essence.

Core Idea
Shared names do not mean shared natures; they mark difference.

Maturidi provides a key principle for understanding divine attributes: the absolute name (al-ism al-mutlaq) — applying the same term to God and creatures — does not entail similarity (tashbih). To prove this, he draws on a universal feature of language: opposites have distinct names. If shared names implied likeness, opposites would be indistinguishable. Read this passage to see how Maturidi grounds a major theological point in linguistic observation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Read the first sentence: 'The absolute name does not admit verification of anthropomorphism.' This is the thesis.
2
Examine the linguistic argument
Maturidi points to opposites like life/death, light/darkness — each has a separate name. If the name itself implied similarity, these opposites would not be distinguished. But they are. So the name alone does not entail likeness.
3
Apply to divine names
Reflect: When we call God 'Living' and creatures 'living', the shared name does not make God like a creature. The name is a sign, not a description of essence.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : } ثم الأصل عندنا أن الاسم المطلق لا يحتمل تحقق التشبيه; لما وُجد كل متضاد في الشاهد تحت الاسم، نحو الحياة والموت، والنور والظلمة والشر والخير، والكفر والإيمان; لكل اسم على حِدَةٍ، فلو كان بالاسم المطلق تشابه لكان لا تضادّ يُعلَم، ولا اختلاف بالأسماء. ثبت أنها جعلت لما يراد من الاختلاف والاتفاق الذي لا يُعلم حقيقة ذلك لو لم يكن له اسم. ولو كان بموافقة الاسم عند نفي المعنى الذي له المعقول من المسمى تشابه في الشاهد لكان لم يُسمَّ للعالم العلوي والسفلي والمبدع
Shaykh Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Then the principle with us is that the absolute name does not admit the verification of anthropomorphism; because every opposite in the witnessed [world] is found under the name, such as life and death, light and darkness, evil and good, disbelief and faith; each name separately. So if there were similarity by the absolute name, then no opposition would be known, and no difference by names. It is established that they were made for what is intended of difference and agreement whose reality would not be known if it had no name. And if there were by the agreement of the name at the negation of the meaning which is understood from the named, similarity in the witnessed, then the upper and lower world and the Originate would not be named.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal principle stated by Imam Maturidi. He argues that the 'absolute name' (الاسم المطلق), i.e., using the same term for God and creatures, does not imply anthropomorphism. The reason is that in the witnessed world, opposite things are subsumed under the same name (e.g., 'life' applies to both living and dead? No, he gives examples like life and death, light and darkness, good and evil, faith and disbelief — each pair has separate names. Actually, he says 'each opposite is under the name' meaning that opposite realities are known by distinct names. If the absolute name (shared naming) implied similarity, then opposites would not be distinguishable by their names. But we do distinguish them. Therefore, the use of the same name for God and creatures does not entail that they are similar; rather, names are instituted to indicate difference and agreement. If the name were to imply similarity when the intended meaning is negated, then the higher and lower worlds and the Creator would not have been named at all. In other words, the shared name is not based on shared nature but on convention denoting distinct realities.

Key Terms
الاسم المطلق
التشبيه
التضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that applying the same word to God and creatures implies anthropomorphism—that God must be like creatures if we use the same attribute.
Correction: Maturidi shows that names do not necessitate shared essence; they are conventional signs that distinguish opposites. The shared name 'Living' for God and a creature does not mean God's life is like creature's life.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the absolute name not imply anthropomorphism?
A) Because God is beyond names
B) Because opposites are named differently, so shared names do not indicate similarity
C) Because the absolute name only applies to the Creator
D) Because names are arbitrary

Key Takeaway
The absolute name does not imply anthropomorphism; names serve to distinguish, not to equate essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Critique of Al-Aslah: Why 'Most Beneficial' Cannot Apply to God
Maturidi critiques al-aslah and redefines wisdom to refute the 'why' question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the concept of al-aslah (the most beneficial) as a condition for God's actions and how this critique undermines the 'why' question.

Core Idea
If 'most beneficial' were a condition for God, it could also be a condition for corruption.

Before Maturidi addresses the 'why' question directly, he first dismantles a common assumption: that God's actions must be 'most beneficial' (al-aslah) for someone. This idea, if applied to God, leads to absurdity. In this passage, you will see how Maturidi turns the logic of al-aslah against itself and redefines wisdom and justice to preserve divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-aslah
Read the Arabic passage (from 'wa-in arada bihi' to 'la yakhruju fi‘luhu ‘an dhalik') and its translation. Notice how the author sets up a critique of the term 'most beneficial'.
The passage assumes the reader knows the Mu‘tazili doctrine that God must do what is most beneficial for creatures.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
Maturidi argues that if al-aslah were a condition for God's act, that same condition could make the act corrupt. Find this argument and reflect on its force.
3
Understand the definitions of wisdom and justice
The passage redefines wisdom as correctness and placing things in their proper place, equating it with justice. See how this definition protects God's actions from being questioned.
Wisdom, in this sense, is not a goal for God but a description of His perfect act.
4
Connect to the 'why' question
Notice that the critique of al-aslah is directly tied to the question 'why did He do?'. The author says the two are equivalent. Reflect on why this is a powerful move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أراد به معنى سواه، فإن القول في معرفة الأصلح كهو في أنه «لِمَ فَعَلَ؟»، سَوَاءٌ. مع ما يُسأل عن شرط الأصلح له في الفعل : من أين يجب؟ على أن أحق الناس بالاستحياء من هم؛ إذ ليس من شيء يُجعل شرطا للأصلح إلا وأمكن أن يكون ذلك شرطا للفساد، ويكون به أعظم الفساد. ولا يجوز أن يكون شيء حكمة يصير سفها؛ لأن تأويل الأصلح أن يكون أصلح لغيره، وقد يكون به الفساد لآخر عندهم. وتأويل الحكمة الإصابة، وهو وضع كل شيء موضعه، وذلك معنى العدل، ولا يخرج فعله عن ذلك. وقال: إن الله خالق بذاته، إذ هو اسم المدح والعظمة، ومحال أن يكون الله سبحانه يستحقه بغيره، لما فيه إيجاب النفع له، ومَنْ ذلك وصف فعله فهو محتاج. وإذ قد ثبت أنه خالق بذاته لم يجز أن لا يكون خالقا ألبتة، والسؤال عن الـ «لِمَ» محال كالسؤل عن «لم قدر ولم علم؟». ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And if He intended by it a meaning other than that, then the statement about knowing the most beneficial (al-aslah) is the same as the statement about 'why did He do?' It is the same. Along with what is asked about the condition of the most beneficial for Him in the act: from where is it obligatory? But the people most deserving of shame are they; for there is nothing that is made a condition for the most beneficial except that it is possible that that condition becomes a condition for corruption, and thereby the greatest corruption occurs. And it is not permissible that something be wisdom and become foolishness; because the interpretation of al-aslah is that it be more beneficial for another, and it may be corruption for another according to them. And the interpretation of wisdom is correctness, and it is placing everything in its proper place, and that is the meaning of justice, and His act does not depart from that. And He said: Indeed Allah is Creator by His essence, for He is the name of praise and greatness, and it is impossible that Allah, exalted, deserves it by other than Him, because it necessitates benefit for Him, and whoever describes His act as such is in need. And since it has been established that He is Creator by His essence, it is not permissible that He not be a Creator at all, and the question about 'why' is invalid, like the question about 'why did He decree and why did He know?' And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the question 'Why did God create?' by deconstructing the concept of al-aslah (the most beneficial) and affirming God's essential attribute of being Creator. The author first shows that applying the notion of 'most beneficial' to God leads to absurdity: if one stipulates a condition for what is most beneficial for God, that condition could equally be a condition for corruption, resulting in the greatest corruption. Moreover, wisdom cannot become foolishness, because the true meaning of al-aslah is benefit for another, not for God, and wisdom is correctness and justice, from which God's act never departs. The author then states a decisive argument: Allah is Creator by His essence (khāliq bi-dhātihi). This means He does not acquire the attribute of Creator from anything else, as that would imply benefit or need. Since this is established, it is impossible for Him not to be Creator always. Therefore, asking 'why' He creates is as meaningless as asking 'why' He decrees or knows. The passage concludes with the formula of reliance on God.

Key Terms
أصلح
حكمة
السؤال عن لم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God must always do what is most beneficial for His creatures, and that we can demand justification for His actions based on this principle.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the concept of al-aslah, if applied to God, leads to absurdity because it could also justify corruption. Moreover, benefit and harm are relative to creatures, not conditions for God. God's actions are inherently wise and just, so asking 'why' is invalid.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem with making 'most beneficial' a condition for God's actions, according to Maturidi?
A) It is impossible for humans to know what is most beneficial for God.
B) The same condition could become a condition for corruption, leading to the greatest corruption.
C) God does not care about benefiting creatures.
D) The Qur'an does not mention 'most beneficial'.

Key Takeaway
The concept of 'most beneficial' cannot be used to question God's actions because it leads to logical absurdity and ignores God's transcendent wisdom and justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a hardship, do you find yourself internally demanding that God explain Himself, as if He owes you the 'most beneficial' outcome? How does this lesson expose that demand as a subtle form of arrogance?
?
The passage says wisdom is placing everything in its proper place. Think of one area of your life where you are convinced God has placed you 'wrongly'—a job, a relationship, a trial. What would it cost you to trust that this is actually the perfect placement, even if you don't see how?
?
If insisting on what you think is 'best' for you could actually open the door to spiritual corruption, where in your life are you clinging to your own definition of 'best' rather than submitting to God's wisdom?

Creator by Essence: Why Asking 'Why' Is Invalid
Maturidi asserts God is Creator by essence and dismisses 'why' questions as invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi asserts that Allah is Creator by His essence and how this makes the question 'Why did God create?' as meaningless as asking 'Why does God have power?'.

Core Idea
If God creates by His very essence, asking 'why' implies need, which is impossible for God.

After critiquing the notion of al-aslah, Maturidi presents a decisive argument based on God's essence. He states that Allah is Creator by His essence (khāliq bi-dhātihi) – a core Maturidi doctrine. This means that creation is not an act He acquires or for which He has a reason; it is inherent to who He is. Therefore, questioning 'why' He creates is like questioning 'why' He knows or decrees. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the essence argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'wa qala: inna Allaha khaliqun bi-dhatihi' to the end, along with its translation. Focus on the logic: God is Creator by essence, so He cannot not be Creator.
The phrase 'khāliq bi-dhātihi' is a technical term meaning the attribute of creation is essential, not acquired.
2
Understand the reasoning
The author explains that 'Creator' is a name of praise and greatness, and God cannot deserve it from another because that would imply benefit or need for Him. See how this connects to self-sufficiency.
If God needed to get the attribute 'Creator' from something else, He would be dependent, which is impossible.
3
Apply the conclusion to the 'why' question
Since God is always Creator by essence, asking 'why' He creates is like asking 'why' He decrees or knows. These questions are invalid because they assume a cause or purpose for what is essential.
The analogy with 'why did He decree and why did He know?' shows the question is category mistake.
4
Reflect on the closing supplication
The passage ends with 'wa la quwwata illa billah'. Consider why this phrase is appropriate here: it expresses reliance on God, acknowledging that all power is from Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

God is Unlike All Creation
Maturidi affirms that nothing resembles God in any aspect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must be absolutely unlike all creation in every aspect.

Core Idea
Nothing resembles God; any resemblance would imply contingency.

Imam Maturidi begins by establishing the fundamental principle of divine unlikeness. He argues that if anything resembled God, it would share the same limitations as creation, which is impossible. This passage lays the foundation for understanding God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Maturidi's argument that nothing resembles God.
Notice the logical flow: if resemblance, then necessity of originatedness or pre-eternity.
2
Identify the key argument
Maturidi states that if something resembled God, it would necessitate either originatedness or pre-eternity, both of which are impossible for God.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Since all creatures share needs, weakness, and originatedness, God must be different in every respect.
This is the proper definition of the Lord.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيعرف أنه جل وعلا لا يشبهه شيء من ذلك، ولا معنى؛ إذ من الوجه الذي يشبهه يوجب ما أوجب فيه من حدث أو قدم، أو تدبير غير فيه وعليه.. وكذلك جميع الأشياء؛ إذ بينها موافقة في الحاجات وأنواع العجز والضعف، ثم في الحدثية من كل الوجوه. فيجب بهذا أن يعرف أنه خلاف له بكل الجهات والجهات له لا لمدبره فيكون في ذلك تعريف الرب بما هو أهله. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى هذا يبطل قول جهم: إنه لم يكن عالماً قادراً ثم صار كذلك، وقول من يقول : لم يكن فاعلاً متكلماً ثم صار كذلك؛ إذ مكنوا فيه تغير الجهات والأحوال التي العبد نفسه خلقاً وحدثاً. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وبما ذكرت من إمكان قبول الأحوال اختياراً، واحتماله الصفات العلية من نحو العلم والقدرة والحياة والسمع والبصر ما يوضح كونه بالصانع العليم، لا بالطبائع التي هي عاجزة عن الاختيار وجاهلة بالأحوال؛ وكذلك جميع الأغذية ولا قوة إلا بالله. وكذلك باحتماله الخير والشر ومختلف الأحوال دليل صرف تدبيره إلى من لا يوصف بالاحتمال ولا بمختلف الأحوال، ليكون كل شيء على ما عليه تقديره له. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقال قوم من عرف نفسه الخفية عرف ربه ونفسه الخفية هي الكيان المجعول لصلاح الأمور، واحتمال المعالي، وملك تدبير الخلائق، ودرك الخفيات من الأمور بالفكر والنظر في الأسباب. وما قاله حسن. وقد يقع بما ذكرت في معرفة الصانع كفاية عن درك الخفي به، بما خفى من أحواله، ووصل إلى العلم بما استتر وظهر بالأسباب. وبه يعرف ما خفى منه، سمي نفساً أولاً وظهر [بعد]. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
So he knows that He, exalted and high, nothing of that resembles Him, nor any meaning; for from the aspect that resembles Him, it necessitates what it necessitates in it of originatedness or pre-eternity, or management different in it and upon it... and likewise all things; for between them there is agreement in needs and types of incapacity and weakness, then in originatedness from every aspect. So by this it must be known that He is different from it in all aspects, and the aspects are for Him not for its manager, so in that there is a definition of the Lord as He deserves. And there is no power except with Allah. And upon this is invalidated the saying of Jahm: that He was not knowing, powerful, then became so, and the saying of one who says: He was not an agent, speaking, then became so; for they allowed in Him change of aspects and states that the servant himself is created and originated. And there is no power except with Allah. And by what I mentioned of the possibility of accepting states by choice, and his bearing the lofty attributes such as knowledge, power, life, hearing, and sight, it clarifies that he is by the wise Maker, not by natural dispositions which are incapable of choice and ignorant of states; and likewise all nutrients. And there is no power except with Allah. And likewise by his bearing good and evil and varying states is evidence for directing his management to One who is not described by bearing nor by varying states, so that everything may be upon what His decree has determined for it. And there is no power except with Allah. And some people said: whoever knows his hidden self knows his Lord, and his hidden self is the constitution made for the rectification of affairs, and bearing of high matters, and the possession of managing creatures, and attaining hidden matters through thought and reflection on causes. And what they said is good. And what I have mentioned may suffice in the knowledge of the Maker from attaining the hidden by it, through what is hidden of his states, and he attains knowledge of what is concealed and apparent through causes. And by it he knows what is hidden from him, it is called soul first and appears [after]. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous proof to affirm God's absolute transcendence and refute incorrect theological views. Imam Maturidi begins by stating that after the previous reasoning, one knows that nothing resembles God at all—neither in external existence nor in conceptual meaning. Any resemblance would imply that God shares contingent attributes like origination or pre-eternity, or would subject Him to differing management. All creatures share needs, weakness, and originatedness, so God must be different in every respect. This definition is what truly characterizes the Lord. Then Maturidi explicitly refutes the Jahmiyya claim that God was not knowing and powerful and then became so, and the similar claim that He was not acting and speaking and then became so. Such views wrongly attribute change to God, which belongs only to created beings. Next, he argues that the human ability to choose and accept varying states, and to possess attributes like knowledge, power, life, hearing, and sight, indicates that humans are made by a wise Creator, not by blind nature (taba'i') which cannot choose or know. The same applies to all sustenance. Additionally, the human capacity for both good and evil and varying states points to a Creator who does not change. Finally, Maturidi mentions the view of some people that knowing the hidden self (al-nafs al-khafiyya) leads to knowing God—this hidden self being the constitution for rectification, bearing high matters, managing creatures, and grasping hidden things through reason. He approves this view but notes that his own earlier explanations suffice for knowing the Maker. One can know what is hidden in oneself through hidden states and manifest causes.

Key Terms
مخالفة (unlikeness)
حدث (origination)
قدم (pre-eternity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God shares attributes with creation, like having a body or changing.
Correction: Maturidi shows that any resemblance would imply contingency, so God is absolutely unlike creation.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why can nothing resemble God?
A) Because God is too great for human understanding
B) Because resemblance would imply originatedness or pre-eternity
C) Because the Quran says so
D) Because humans cannot imagine Him

Key Takeaway
God is absolutely unlike all creation in every aspect, and this is the proper definition of the Lord.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do I ever mentally picture God in a way that resembles something from creation, even if I verbally deny it? What if I am unknowingly forming an image based on my own understanding? Am I willing to actively purify my conception of God by constantly reminding myself that He is absolutely unlike anything I can imagine, and that any thought of resemblance is actually a failure to honor Him as He deserves?
?
The passage states that all creatures share needs, weakness, and originatedness. When I feel self-sufficient or proud, am I forgetting that I am entirely contingent and weak before God? How can I use this truth to cultivate humility and reliance on Him, and to ensure that my worship is directed to the One who is completely beyond my comprehension?
?
If God is utterly unlike any of my experiences, then my relationship with Him must be based solely on what He reveals. Is there any aspect of my faith where I am relying on my own assumptions or cultural ideas about God rather than on revelation? Will I commit to re-examining my beliefs about God and discarding any that are not grounded in the Quran and sound reasoning, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Divine Transcendence
beginner
4 min
Refutation of Jahm's Claim of Change in God
Maturidi refutes Jahm's claim that God acquired attributes over time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Jahm's claim that God acquired attributes is invalid.

Core Idea
Change in attributes belongs to creation, not to the eternal God.

Maturidi explicitly refutes the Jahmiyya heresy that God was not knowing or powerful and then became so. Such a view implies that God undergoes change, which is a characteristic of created beings. This passage protects divine immutability.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read Maturidi's words: 'Upon this is invalidated the saying of Jahm...' and understand the claims being rejected.
Jahm claimed God acquired knowledge and power over time.
2
Identify the error in Jahm's view
Maturidi says such claims allow change in God's aspects and states, which are only for created beings.
The servant (human) is created and originated, hence changeable.
3
Understand the implication
If God changes, He would be like creation, contradicting His eternity and perfection.
This is why Jahm's view is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What does Maturidi say about Jahm's view that God was not knowing then became knowing?
A) It is possible if God wills

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself imagining that God's love for you increases when you obey and decreases when you sin, as if His attributes are subject to change? How does this lesson call you to repentance from such a view?
?
When you consider your own constant changes—your moods, your knowledge, your abilities—how does the reality of God's unchanging attributes humble you and deepen your trust in Him today?
?
If God were subject to change like His creation, how would that affect your confidence in His promises? What specific step will you take this week to anchor your heart in His eternal, unchanging nature?

Divine Transcendence

5 min
Human Attributes Point to a Wise Creator
Maturidi argues that human attributes require a wise Creator, not nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how human attributes like knowledge and choice prove a wise Creator.

Core Idea
Our ability to choose and possess lofty attributes comes from a wise Maker, not blind nature.

Maturidi presents a powerful argument from design: humans possess attributes like knowledge, power, life, hearing, and sight, and they can choose and accept varying states. Such complex capacities cannot arise from natural dispositions (taba'i'), which lack choice and knowledge. This points to an all-wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the passage listing human attributes and the conclusion that they come from the wise Maker.
The attributes mentioned are knowledge, power, life, hearing, and sight.
2
Compare with natural dispositions
Nature is incapable of choice and ignorant of states, so it cannot produce such capacities.
This refutes materialist or atheistic explanations.
3
Apply to all sustenance
Maturidi extends the argument to all nutrients, showing the same principle.
Everything points back to the Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Why does Maturidi argue that human attributes cannot come from natural dispositions?
A) Because nature is beautiful
B) Because nature is incapable of choice and ignorant of states
C) Because humans are made of clay
D) Because the Quran denies it

Human capacities like choice and lofty attributes are clear signs of a wise Creator, not blind nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own capacity to choose and learn, do you habitually recognize it as a direct gift from the wise Maker, or do you take it for granted? What would change if you consciously thanked Allah for your ability to think and decide every time you used it?
?
Maturidi says nature is 'incapable of choice and ignorant of states.' Yet you are not ignorant; you have knowledge and hearing and sight. If these did not come from Allah, can you honestly imagine where else they could have come? How would your life be different if you lived with the certainty that every breath is a sign pointing to the Creator?
?
The lesson ends with 'There is no power except with Allah.' Do you truly feel this reliance in your daily actions, or do you often rely on your own strength? What would it look like to pause before every major decision and say, 'La quwwata illa billah'?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
6 min
Bearing Good and Evil and Knowing the Hidden Self
Maturidi uses human experience of opposites and the hidden self to argue for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how human experience of good and evil points to a transcendent God, and how knowing one's hidden self leads to knowing Him.

Core Idea
Our experience of opposites and our inner self both testify to a Creator beyond change.

Imam Maturidi presents two further proofs: first, the human capacity to bear both good and evil and varying states indicates that the management of all things belongs to a God who is beyond such variation. Second, he endorses the view that knowing one's hidden self (al-nafs al-khafiyya) leads to knowing God. This lesson explores these profound arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument
Read about bearing good and evil as evidence for a transcendent God who does not change.
If God changed like we do, He would not be the absolute manager.
2
Read the second argument
Read the saying about the hidden self and Maturidi's approval.
The hidden self is the constitution for managing affairs and grasping hidden truths.
3
Reflect on the sufficiency
Maturidi notes that his earlier proofs are sufficient, but this is a good additional pointer.
He is not relying solely on this inner knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيعرف أنه جل وعلا لا يشبهه شيء من ذلك، ولا معنى؛ إذ من الوجه الذي يشبهه يوجب ما أوجب فيه من حدث أو قدم، أو تدبير غير فيه وعليه.. وكذلك جميع الأشياء؛ إذ بينها موافقة في الحاجات وأنواع العجز والضعف، ثم في الحدثية من كل الوجوه. فيجب بهذا أن يعرف أنه خلاف له بكل الجهات والجهات له لا لمدبره فيكون في ذلك تعريف الرب بما هو أهله. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى هذا يبطل قول جهم: إنه لم يكن عالماً قادراً ثم صار كذلك، وقول من يقول : لم يكن فاعلاً متكلماً ثم صار كذلك؛ إذ مكنوا فيه تغير الجهات والأحوال التي العبد نفسه خلقاً وحدثاً. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وبما ذكرت من إمكان قبول الأحوال اختياراً، واحتماله الصفات العلية من نحو العلم والقدرة والحياة والسمع والبصر ما يوضح كونه بالصانع العليم، لا بالطبائع التي هي عاجزة عن الاختيار وجاهلة بالأحوال؛ وكذلك جميع الأغذية ولا قوة إلا بالله. وكذلك باحتماله الخير والشر ومختلف الأحوال دليل صرف تدبيره إلى من لا يوصف بالاحتمال ولا بمختلف الأحوال، ليكون كل شيء على ما عليه تقديره له. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقال قوم من عرف نفسه الخفية عرف ربه ونفسه الخفية هي الكيان المجعول لصلاح الأمور، واحتمال المعالي، وملك تدبير الخلائق، ودرك الخفيات من الأمور بالفكر والنظر في الأسباب. وما قاله حسن. وقد يقع بما ذكرت في معرفة الصانع كفاية عن درك الخفي به، بما خفى من أحواله، ووصل إلى العلم بما استتر وظهر بالأسباب. وبه يعرف ما خفى منه، سمي نفساً أولاً وظهر [بعد]. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
So he knows that He, exalted and high, nothing of that resembles Him, nor any meaning; for from the aspect that resembles Him, it necessitates what it necessitates in it of originatedness or pre-eternity, or management different in it and upon it... and likewise all things; for between them there is agreement in needs and types of incapacity and weakness, then in originatedness from every aspect. So by this it must be known that He is different from it in all aspects, and the aspects are for Him not for its manager, so in that there is a definition of the Lord as He deserves. And there is no power except with Allah. And upon this is invalidated the saying of Jahm: that He was not knowing, powerful, then became so, and the saying of one who says: He was not an agent, speaking, then became so; for they allowed in Him change of aspects and states that the servant himself is created and originated. And there is no power except with Allah. And by what I mentioned of the possibility of accepting states by choice, and his bearing the lofty attributes such as knowledge, power, life, hearing, and sight, it clarifies that he is by the wise Maker, not by natural dispositions which are incapable of choice and ignorant of states; and likewise all nutrients. And there is no power except with Allah. And likewise by his bearing good and evil and varying states is evidence for directing his management to One who is not described by bearing nor by varying states, so that everything may be upon what His decree has determined for it. And there is no power except with Allah. And some people said: whoever knows his hidden self knows his Lord, and his hidden self is the constitution made for the rectification of affairs, and bearing of high matters, and the possession of managing creatures, and attaining hidden matters through thought and reflection on causes. And what they said is good. And what I have mentioned may suffice in the knowledge of the Maker from attaining the hidden by it, through what is hidden of his states, and he attains knowledge of what is concealed and apparent through causes. And by it he knows what is hidden from him, it is called soul first and appears [after]. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous proof to affirm God's absolute transcendence and refute incorrect theological views. Imam Maturidi begins by stating that after the previous reasoning, one knows that nothing resembles God at all—neither in external existence nor in conceptual meaning. Any resemblance would imply that God shares contingent attributes like origination or pre-eternity, or would subject Him to differing management. All creatures share needs, weakness, and originatedness, so God must be different in every respect. This definition is what truly characterizes the Lord. Then Maturidi explicitly refutes the Jahmiyya claim that God was not knowing and powerful and then became so, and the similar claim that He was not acting and speaking and then became so. Such views wrongly attribute change to God, which belongs only to created beings. Next, he argues that the human ability to choose and accept varying states, and to possess attributes like knowledge, power, life, hearing, and sight, indicates that humans are made by a wise Creator, not by blind nature (taba'i') which cannot choose or know. The same applies to all sustenance. Additionally, the human capacity for both good and evil and varying states points to a Creator who does not change. Finally, Maturidi mentions the view of some people that knowing the hidden self (al-nafs al-khafiyya) leads to knowing God—this hidden self being the constitution for rectification, bearing high matters, managing creatures, and grasping hidden things through reason. He approves this view but notes that his own earlier explanations suffice for knowing the Maker. One can know what is hidden in oneself through hidden states and manifest causes.

Key Terms
نفس خفية (hidden self)
تدبير (management)
خير وشر (good and evil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think knowing oneself is sufficient without revelation or external proofs.
Correction: Maturidi uses it as a supplementary proof but affirms that his earlier arguments are sufficient.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the hidden self?
A) It is the only way to know God
B) It is a good means to know God, but earlier proofs suffice
C) It is a dangerous concept
D) It is the same as the physical body

Key Takeaway
Human experience of opposites and knowledge of the hidden self both point to a transcendent Creator who is beyond change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You bear both good and evil, joy and sorrow, every day. In the midst of your changing states, how often do you consciously turn your heart to the One who never changes? Will you commit to a daily moment of reflection where you trace a recent experience of variation back to the unchanging Creator?
?
Your hidden self—your capacity to think, manage, and seek hidden truths—is a gift from God intended to lead you to know Him. When did you last deliberately use your intellect to ponder the signs of God within yourself? Starting today, will you set aside time to reflect on your own inner constitution as evidence of the Creator?
?
Maturidi said that knowing the hidden self suffices for knowledge of the Maker. But do you truly know your own soul—its motives, its aspirations, its limitations? What hidden assumptions or distractions keep you from using this self-knowledge to deepen your relationship with God? What will you do this week to uncover one hidden truth about yourself that points back to Him?

God's Transcendence and Refutation of Jahm
Maturidi proves God's dissimilarity to creation and refutes Jahm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot resemble creation and how Maturidi refutes the claim that God's attributes change over time.

Core Idea
God is completely different from creation in every respect.

Maturidi lays down a fundamental principle: nothing in creation resembles God. He then shows how any similarity would force unacceptable implications, and concludes by rejecting views that attribute change to God. This passage establishes both a positive doctrine of transcendence and a critical refutation of anthropomorphic tendencies.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without interpreting. Notice how Maturidi begins with a conclusion (فيعرف) and then provides reasoning.
The passage uses conditional reasoning.
2
Understand the argument against resemblance
Maturidi says if there were any similarity, it would imply that God shares properties like temporal origination or pre-eternity with creation, which is impossible.
3
Observe the universal traits of creation
All created things share needs, incapacity, weakness, and temporality. This shows their common nature.
4
Grasp the conclusion of dissociation
Therefore, God is different in every respect. The attributes of perfection belong to Him alone.
5
Examine the refutation of Jahm
Maturidi refutes those who claim God's attributes (knowledge, power, action, speech) can change. Such change would make God like created beings.
This refutation relies on the earlier principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيعرف أنه جل وعلا لا يُشبهه شيء من ذلك، ولا معنى؛ إذ من الوجه الذي يشبهه يوجب ما أوجب فيه من حدث أو قدم، أو تدبير غير فيه وعليه.. وكذلك جميع الأشياء؛ إذ بينها موافقة في الحاجات وأنواع العجز والضعف، ثم في الحدثيّة من كل الوجوه. فيجب بهذا أن يعرف أنه خلاف له بكل الجهات والجهات له لا لمدبّره فيكون في ذلك تعريف الرب بما هو أهله. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى هذا يبطل قول جهم: إنه لم يكن عالماً قادرًا ثم صار كذلك، وقول من يقول: لم يكن فاعلاً متكلماً ثم صار كذلك؛ إذ مكنوا فيه تغير الجهات والأحوال التي العبد نفسه خلقاً وحدثاً. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
So it is known that He, may He be glorified and exalted, is not resembled by any of that, and there is no meaning; because from the aspect that resembles Him it necessitates what it necessitates in it of temporal origination or pre-eternity, or a management that changes in it and upon it... And likewise all things; because among them there is agreement in needs and types of incapacity and weakness, then in temporality from every aspect. So by this it must be known that He is different from it in every respect, and the respects are for Him, not for its manager, so in that there is a definition of the Lord as befits Him. And there is no power except through Allah. And upon this is refuted the statement of Jahm: that He was not knowing and powerful then became so, and the statement of those who say: He was not an agent and speaker then became so; because they posited in Him a change of respects and states that the servant himself is created and temporal. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. Maturidi begins by establishing that God cannot resemble any created thing. If there were any similarity, it would imply that the created thing's properties—such as temporal origination or pre-eternity, or management that changes—would also apply to God, which is impossible. He observes that all created things share needs, incapacity, and weakness, and are all temporal in every way. Therefore, God must be completely different from them in every respect, and all attributes of perfection belong to Him alone. This gives a proper definition of the Lord. He then refutes Jahm, who claimed that God was not knowing and powerful then became so, and others who said He was not an agent and speaker then became so. Such views wrongly attribute change to God, which is a characteristic of created beings, not the Creator.

Key Terms
شبه
حدث
قدم
جهة
جهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God was not knowing and powerful but later became so, or that His attributes can change over time.
Correction: Maturidi argues that such change would make God like creation, because all created things are subject to change. God is eternal and unchanging.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would be implied if God resembled creation?
A) God would be temporal
B) God would share properties like temporal origination or pre-eternity
C) God would be powerful
D) God would be the manager

Key Takeaway
God is absolutely different from creation in every respect, and any view implying change in God is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever imagine God in ways that resemble human characteristics—having moods, changing plans, or acquiring new knowledge? How does Maturidi's argument that God is different from creation in every respect challenge those mental images? What will you actively do today to purify your concept of God?
?
Jahm claimed God was not knowing then became knowing. Are there any attributes of God that you secretly think of as acquired or changing over time? How does this passage expose that as a false projection of createdness onto the Creator? Commit to one specific way you will remember God's eternal perfection this week.
?
This passage says that any view implying change in God is false because change is a mark of creation. Examine your own speech and supplications: do you ever speak of God as if He 'decides' or 'changes His mind' in reaction to situations? What step can you take to ensure your words about God remain free from such implications?

Why 'Body' Cannot Be Applied to God
Maturidi refutes applying 'body' to God by showing it entails limit and direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi refutes applying the term 'body' (جسم) to God based on the concepts of limit (حد) and direction (جهة).

Core Idea
Body implies limit and direction; God transcends both.

Maturidi addresses a key theological error: applying the term 'body' (جسم) to God. He argues that 'body' necessarily implies limitation and direction, which are incompatible with God's transcendence. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and clarifies the distinction between 'thing' and 'body'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi builds his argument step by step.
2
Identify the definition of 'body'
Maturidi begins by defining 'body' as a name for everything limited. This is key to his refutation.
Contrast body with 'thing' (شيء), which is mere affirmation.
3
Understand the distinction with 'thing'
The world's existence provides evidence for affirmation, so the term 'thing' can be used for God. But the world is finite under a limit, which proves God has no limit.
4
Analyze the argument about limit
The term 'limit' usually denotes physical ends. Maturidi notes that if 'limit' means oneness or lordship, it can be accepted, but otherwise it is negated of God.
5
Consider the implication of directions
Body in the visible world entails directions, each direction being variable. This is impossible for God, so the claim that God is a body is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجسم فهو اسم لكل محدود والشيء إثبات لا غير. وفي وجود العالم على ما عليه دليل الإثبات؛ لذلك قيل بالشيء، وفيه ـ إذ هو متناه لا من حيث الشيئية [بل من حيث هو] تحت الحد ـ دليل نفي الحد عن الله جل ثناؤه إلا أن يُراد بالحد الوحدانية والربوبية، فهو كذلك، وحرف الحد ساقط؛ لأنه يغلب في الدلالة على نهاية الشيء من طريق العرض ونحو ذلك مما يتعالى عن ذلك، وذلك معنى الجسم في الشاهد. وفيه أيضاً إيجاب الجهات المحتمل كل جهة أن يكون أطول منها وأعرض وأقصر. فلذلك بطل القول بذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And as for 'body', it is a name for everything limited, and 'thing' is affirmation only. And in the existence of the world as it is, there is evidence of affirmation; therefore, it is said with 'thing', and in it—since it is finite not from the aspect of thingness [but from the aspect that it is] under limit—there is evidence of negating limit from Allah, glory be to His praise, unless what is meant by limit is oneness and lordship, then it is so, and the letter of limit is dropped; because it overwhelmingly indicates the end of a thing in the way of accident and the like, which He is exalted above, and that is the meaning of 'body' in witnessed things. And in it also is the necessity of directions, each direction potentially being longer, wider, and shorter than another. Therefore, the statement with that is invalid. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage refutes applying 'body' (الجسم) to God. Maturidi defines body as a name for everything limited. In contrast, 'thing' is mere affirmation. The world's existence proves affirmation (of God), so 'thing' is used for God. But the world is finite due to being under a limit, which proves that God is beyond limit—unless 'limit' is interpreted as oneness and lordship, which are true. The term 'limit' is usually used for physical ends, which God transcends. Body in witnessed things implies directions, each direction being longer, wider, or shorter. This is impossible for God, so the claim that God is a body is invalid.

Key Terms

محدود

جهة
شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God has a body like humans because scripture describes Him with body parts.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'body' necessarily entails limit and direction, which cannot apply to God. Scriptural descriptions are metaphorical or interpreted in a way befitting His transcendence.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say the term 'body' cannot be applied to God?
A) Because God is not physical
B) Because it implies limit and direction
C) Because it is a linguistic mistake
D) Because the Quran does not use it

Key Takeaway
Body is defined as limited and implying direction; therefore, it cannot be applied to God, who transcends all limits and directions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does Maturidi's argument challenge that hidden assumption? What would it mean for you to truly believe that God transcends all direction and limit, even in your deepest thoughts?
?
The passage says that body necessarily implies direction, and each direction can be longer or shorter—this variability is incompatible with God's perfection. Have you ever considered that even thinking of God as 'above' us in a literal sense reduces Him to something less than perfect? Are you willing to let go of any spatial images you hold of God, even if they comfort you?
?
Maturidi distinguishes between 'body' and 'thing'. He says 'thing' is affirmation only. In your worship, do you affirm God's existence without trying to picture Him? Can you sincerely say 'there is nothing like unto Him' not just with your tongue, but with your heart's imagination? What practical step can you take today to purify your conception of God?

Divine Transcendence
